## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The>The</a> 

<font size=3>
The (/Ã°É™, Ã°iË/ (listen)) is a grammatical article in English, denoting persons or things already mentioned, under discussion, implied or otherwise presumed familiar to listeners, readers, or speakers. ã€€It is the definite article in English. ã€€The is the most frequently used word in the English language; studies and analyses of texts have found it to account for seven percent of all printed English-language words. ã€€It is derived from gendered articles in Old English which combined in Middle English and now has a single form used with pronouns of any gender. ã€€The word can be used with both singular and plural nouns, and with a noun that starts with any letter. ã€€This is different from many other languages, which have different forms of the definite article for different genders or numbers.
</font>

<b>to</b> | tu: | prep. The preposition to primarily indicates approach and arrival,<br> motion made in the direction of a place or thing and attaining it,<br> access; and also, motion or tendency without arrival; movement toward;<br> -- opposed to from.<br>prep. Hence, it indicates motion, course, or tendency toward a<br> time, a state or condition, an aim, or anything capable of being<br> regarded as a limit to a tendency, movement, or action; as, he is going<br> to a trade; he is rising to wealth and honor.<br>prep. In a very general way, and with innumerable varieties of<br> application, to connects transitive verbs with their remoter or<br> indirect object, and adjectives, nouns, and neuter or passive verbs<br> with a following noun which limits their action. Its sphere verges upon<br> that of for, but it contains less the idea of design or appropriation;<br> as, these remarks were addressed to a large audience; let us keep this<br> seat to ourselves; a substance sweet to the taste; an event painful to<br> the mind; duty to God and to our parents; a dislike to spirituous<br> liquor.<br>prep. As sign of the infinitive, to had originally the use of last<br> defined, governing the infinitive as a verbal noun, and connecting it<br> as indirect object with a preceding verb or adjective; thus, ready to<br> go, i.e., ready unto going; good to eat, i.e., good for eating; I do my<br> utmost to lead my life pleasantly. But it has come to be the almost<br> constant prefix to the infinitive, even in situations where it has no<br> prepositional meaning, as where the infinitive is direct object or<br> subject; thus, I love to learn, i.e., I love learning; to die for one's<br> country is noble, i.e., the dying for one's country. Where the<br> infinitive denotes the design or purpose, good usage formerly allowed<br> the prefixing of for to the to; as, what went ye out for see? (Matt.<br> xi. 8).<br>prep. In many phrases, and in connection with many other words, to<br> has a pregnant meaning, or is used elliptically.<br>prep. Extent; limit; degree of comprehension; inclusion as far as;<br> as, they met us to the number of three hundred.<br>prep. Effect; end; consequence; as, the prince was flattered to<br> his ruin; he engaged in a war to his cost; violent factions exist to<br> the prejudice of the state.<br>prep. Apposition; connection; antithesis; opposition; as, they<br> engaged hand to hand.<br>prep. Accord; adaptation; as, an occupation to his taste; she has<br> a husband to her mind.<br>prep. Comparison; as, three is to nine as nine is to twenty-seven;<br> it is ten to one that you will offend him.<br>prep. Addition; union; accumulation.<br>prep. Accompaniment; as, she sang to his guitar; they danced to<br> the music of a piano.<br>prep. Character; condition of being; purpose subserved or office<br> filled. | <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/to>prep. åˆ°, å‘, è¶‹äº<br>adv. å‘å‰</a> |  |  | 1.73

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A>A</a> 

<font size=3>
A, or a, is the first letter and the first vowel of the Latin alphabet, used in the modern English alphabet, the alphabets of other western European languages and others worldwide. ã€€Its name in English is a (pronounced /ËˆeÉª/), plural aes. ã€€It is similar in shape to the Ancient Greek letter alpha, from which it derives. ã€€The uppercase version consists of the two slanting sides of a triangle, crossed in the middle by a horizontal bar. ã€€The lowercase version can be written in two forms: the double-storey a and single-storey É‘. ã€€The latter is commonly used in handwriting and fonts based on it, especially fonts intended to be read by children, and is also found in italic type. ã€€In English grammar, "a", and its variant "an", are indefinite articles.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/That>That</a> 

<font size=3>
That is an English language word used for several grammatical purposes. ã€€These include use as an adjective, conjunction, pronoun, adverb, and intensifier; it has distance from the speaker, as opposed to words like this. ã€€The word did not originally exist in Old English, and its concept was represented by Ã¾e. ã€€Once it came into being, it was spelt as Ã¾Ã¦t (among others, such as Ã¾et), taking the role of the modern that. ã€€It also took on the role of the modern word what, though this has since changed, and that has recently replaced some usage of the modern which. ã€€Pronunciation of the word varies according to its role within a sentence, with two main varieties (a strong and a weak form), though there are also regional differences, such as where the /Ã°/ sound is substituted instead by a /d/ in English spoken in Cameroon.
</font>

<b>that</b> | Ã°Ã¦t | pron., a., conj., & As a demonstrative pronoun (pl. Those),<br> that usually points out, or refers to, a person or thing previously<br> mentioned, or supposed to be understood. That, as a demonstrative, may<br> precede the noun to which it refers; as, that which he has said is<br> true; those in the basket are good apples.<br>pron., a., conj., & As an adjective, that has the same<br> demonstrative force as the pronoun, but is followed by a noun.<br>pron., a., conj., & As a relative pronoun, that is<br> equivalent to who or which, serving to point out, and make definite, a<br> person or thing spoken of, or alluded to, before, and may be either<br> singular or plural.<br>pron., a., conj., & As a conjunction, that retains much of<br> its force as a demonstrative pronoun.<br>pron., a., conj., & To introduce a clause employed as the<br> object of the preceding verb, or as the subject or predicate nominative<br> of a verb.<br>pron., a., conj., & To introduce, a reason or cause; --<br> equivalent to for that, in that, for the reason that, because.<br>pron., a., conj., & To introduce a purpose; -- usually<br> followed by may, or might, and frequently preceded by so, in order, to<br> the end, etc.<br>pron., a., conj., & To introduce a consequence, result, or<br> effect; -- usually preceded by so or such, sometimes by that.<br>pron., a., conj., & In an elliptical sentence to introduce<br> a dependent sentence expressing a wish, or a cause of surprise,<br> indignation, or the like.<br>pron., a., conj., & As adverb: To such a degree; so; as, he<br> was that frightened he could say nothing. | <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/that>a. é‚£, é‚£ä¸ª<br>conj. ä»¥è‡´, å› ä¸º<br>pron. é‚£<br>adv. é‚£ä¹ˆ, é‚£æ ·</a> |  |  | 2.18

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Were>Were</a> 

<font size=3>
Look up wer#English in Wiktionary, the free dictionary. ã€€Were and wer are archaic terms for adult male humans and were often used for alliteration with wife as "were and wife" in Germanic-speaking cultures (Old English: wer, Old Dutch: wer, Gothic: waÃ­r, Old Frisian: wer, Old Saxon: wer, Old High German: wer, Old Norse: verr). ã€€In Anglo-Saxon law wer was the value of a man's life. ã€€He could be required to pay his wer to the king as a penalty for crime. ã€€If he was murdered then his relatives were entitled to his wergild as compensation from the murderer.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/T>T</a> 

<font size=3>
This article contains uncommon Unicode characters. ã€€Without proper rendering support, you may see question marks, boxes, or other symbols instead of the intended characters. ã€€T, or t, is the twentieth letter in the Latin alphabet, used in the modern English alphabet, the alphabets of other western European languages and others worldwide. ã€€Its name in English is tee (pronounced /ËˆtiË/), plural tees. ã€€It is derived from the Semitic Taw ğ¤• of the Phoenician and Paleo-Hebrew script (Aramaic and Hebrew Taw ×ª/ğ¡•/, Syriac Taw Ü¬, and Arabic Øª TÄÊ¼) via the Greek letter Ï„ (tau). ã€€In English, it is most commonly used to represent the voiceless alveolar plosive, a sound it also denotes in the International Phonetic Alphabet. ã€€It is the most commonly used consonant and the second-most commonly used letter in English-language texts.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Me_(mythology)>Me (mythology)</a> 

<font size=3>
In Sumerian mythology, a me (ğ’ˆ¨; Sumerian: me; Akkadian: parÅ¡u) is one of the decrees of the divine that is foundational to those social institutions, religious practices, technologies, behaviors, mores, and human conditions that make civilization, as the Sumerians understood it, possible. ã€€They are fundamental to the Sumerian understanding of the relationship between humanity and the gods.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time>Time</a> 

<font size=3>
Time is the continued sequence of existence and events that occurs in an apparently irreversible succession from the past, through the present, into the future. ã€€It is a component quantity of various measurements used to sequence events, to compare the duration of events or the intervals between them, and to quantify rates of change of quantities in material reality or in the conscious experience. ã€€Time is often referred to as a fourth dimension, along with three spatial dimensions. ã€€Time has long been an important subject of study in religion, philosophy, and science, but defining it in a manner applicable to all fields without circularity has consistently eluded scholars. ã€€Nevertheless, diverse fields such as business, industry, sports, the sciences, and the performing arts all incorporate some notion of time into their respective measuring systems. ã€€Time in physics is operationally defined as "what a clock reads". ã€€The physical nature of time is addressed by general relativity with respect to events in spacetime. ã€€Examples of events are the collision of two particles, the explosion of a supernova, or the arrival of a rocket ship. ã€€Every event can be assigned four numbers representing its time and position (the event's coordinates). ã€€However, the numerical values are different for different observers. ã€€In general relativity, the question of what time it is now only has meaning relative to a particular observer. ã€€Distance and time are intimately related, and the time required for light to travel a specific distance is the same for all observers, as first publicly demonstrated by Michelson and Morley. ã€€General relativity does not address the nature of time for extremely small intervals where quantum mechanics holds. ã€€At this time, there is no generally accepted theory of quantum general relativity. ã€€Time is one of the seven fundamental physical quantities in both the International System of Units (SI) and International System of Quantities. ã€€The SI base unit of time is the second, which is defined by measuring the electronic transition frequency of caesium atoms. ã€€Time is used to define other quantities, such as velocity, so defining time in terms of such quantities would result in circularity of definition. ã€€An operational definition of time, wherein one says that observing a certain number of repetitions of one or another standard cyclical event (such as the passage of a free-swinging pendulum) constitutes one standard unit such as the second, is highly useful in the conduct of both advanced experiments and everyday affairs of life. ã€€To describe observations of an event, a location (position in space) and time are typically noted. ã€€The operational definition of time does not address what the fundamental nature of time is. ã€€It does not address why events can happen forward and backward in space, whereas events only happen in the forward progress of time. ã€€Investigations into the relationship between space and time led physicists to define the spacetime continuum. ã€€General relativity is the primary framework for understanding how spacetime works. ã€€Through advances in both theoretical and experimental investigations of spacetime, it has been shown that time can be distorted and dilated, particularly at the edges of black holes. ã€€Temporal measurement has occupied scientists and technologists and was a prime motivation in navigation and astronomy. ã€€Periodic events and periodic motion have long served as standards for units of time. ã€€Examples include the apparent motion of the sun across the sky, the phases of the moon, and the swing of a pendulum. ã€€Time is also of significant social importance, having economic value ("time is money") as well as personal value, due to an awareness of the limited time in each day and in human life spans. ã€€There are many systems for determining what time it is, including the Global Positioning System, other satellite systems, Coordinated Universal Time and mean solar time. ã€€In general, the numbers obtained from different time systems differ from one another.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Than>Than</a> 

<font size=3>
Look up than in Wiktionary, the free dictionary. ã€€Than is a grammatical particle analyzed as both a conjunction and a preposition in the English language. ã€€It introduces a comparison and is associated with comparatives and with words such as more, less, and fewer. ã€€Typically, it measures the force of an adjective or similar description between two predicates.
</font>

It is usually followed by the<br> object compared in the nominative case. Sometimes, however, the object<br> compared is placed in the objective case, and than is then considered<br> by some grammarians as a preposition. Sometimes the object is expressed<br> in a sentence, usually introduced by that; as, I would rather suffer<br> than that you should want.<br>adv. Then. ## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Well>Well</a> 

<font size=3>
A well is an excavation or structure created in the ground by digging, driving, or drilling to access liquid resources, usually water. ã€€The oldest and most common kind of well is a water well, to access groundwater in underground aquifers. ã€€The well water is drawn up by a pump, or using containers, such as buckets or large water bags that are raised mechanically or by hand. ã€€Water can also be injected back into the aquifer through the well. ã€€Wells were first constructed at least eight thousand years ago and historically vary in construction from a simple scoop in the sediment of a dry watercourse to the qanats of Iran, and the stepwells and sakiehs of India. ã€€Placing a lining in the well shaft helps create stability, and linings of wood or wickerwork date back at least as far as the Iron Age. ã€€Wells have traditionally been sunk by hand digging, as is still the case in rural areas of the developing world. ã€€These wells are inexpensive and low-tech as they use mostly manual labour, and the structure can be lined with brick or stone as the excavation proceeds. ã€€A more modern method called caissoning uses pre-cast reinforced concrete well rings that are lowered into the hole. ã€€Driven wells can be created in unconsolidated material with a well hole structure, which consists of a hardened drive point and a screen of perforated pipe, after which a pump is installed to collect the water. ã€€Deeper wells can be excavated by hand drilling methods or machine drilling, using a bit in a borehole. ã€€Drilled wells are usually cased with a factory-made pipe composed of steel or plastic. ã€€Drilled wells can access water at much greater depths than dug wells. ã€€Two broad classes of well are shallow or unconfined wells completed within the uppermost saturated aquifer at that location, and deep or confined wells, sunk through an impermeable stratum into an aquifer beneath. ã€€A collector well can be constructed adjacent to a freshwater lake or stream with water percolating through the intervening material. ã€€The site of a well can be selected by a hydrogeologist, or groundwater surveyor. ã€€Water may be pumped or hand drawn. ã€€Impurities from the surface can easily reach shallow sources and contamination of the supply by pathogens or chemical contaminants needs to be avoided. ã€€Well water typically contains more minerals in solution than surface water and may require treatment before being potable. ã€€Soil salination can occur as the water table falls and the surrounding soil begins to dry out. ã€€Another environmental problem is the potential for methane to seep into the water.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Will_(sociology)>Will (sociology)</a> 

<font size=3>
In sociology, will is a concept introduced by Ferdinand TÃ¶nnies in "Gemeinschaft und Gesellschaft", 1887. ã€€TÃ¶nnies' approach was very much indebted to Spinoza's dictum voluntas atque intellectus unum et idem sunt ("will as well as ratio are one and the same"), and to Arthur Schopenhauer. ã€€TÃ¶nnies saw a fundamental cleavage between "essential will" (Wesenwille) creating community (Gemeinschaft), and "arbitrary will" (KÃ¼rwille), creating society (Gesellschaft) - see Ferdinand TÃ¶nnies.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/May>May</a> 

<font size=3>
May is the fifth month of the year in the Julian and Gregorian calendars and is the third of seven months to have a length of 31 days. ã€€May is a month of spring in the Northern Hemisphere, and autumn in the Southern Hemisphere. ã€€Therefore, May in the Southern Hemisphere is the seasonal equivalent of November in the Northern Hemisphere and vice versa. ã€€Late May typically marks the start of the summer vacation season in the United States (Memorial Day) and Canada (Victoria Day) that ends on Labor Day, the first Monday of September. ã€€May (in Latin, Maius) was named for the Greek goddess Maia, who was identified with the Roman era goddess of fertility, Bona Dea, whose festival was held in May. ã€€Conversely, the Roman poet Ovid provides a second etymology, in which he says that the month of May is named for the maiores, Latin for "elders," and that the following month (June) is named for the iuniores, or "young people" (Fasti VI.88). ã€€Eta Aquariids meteor shower appears in May. ã€€It is visible from about April 21 to about May 20 each year with peak activity on or around May ã€€6. The Arietids shower from May 22 â€“ July 2, and peaks on June ã€€7. The Virginids also shower at various dates in May.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/While>While</a> 

<font size=3>
Look up whileÂ or whilst in Wiktionary, the free dictionary. ã€€While is a word in the English language that functions both as a noun and as a subordinating conjunction. ã€€Its meaning varies largely based on its intended function, position in the phrase and even the writer or speaker's regional dialect. ã€€As a conjunction, it is synonymous with the word whilst, a form often considered archaic in American English, as well as in some style guides on both sides of the Atlantic.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/School>School</a> 

<font size=3>
A school is an educational institution designed to provide learning spaces and learning environments for the teaching of students under the direction of teachers. ã€€Most countries have systems of formal education, which is sometimes compulsory. ã€€In these systems, students progress through a series of schools. ã€€The names for these schools vary by country (discussed in the Regional terms section below) but generally include primary school for young children and secondary school for teenagers who have completed primary education. ã€€An institution where higher education is taught is commonly called a university college or university. ã€€In addition to these core schools, students in a given country may also attend schools before and after primary (elementary in the U.S.) and secondary (middle school in the U.S.) education. ã€€Kindergarten or preschool provide some schooling to very young children (typically ages 3â€“5). ã€€University, vocational school, college or seminary may be available after secondary school. ã€€A school may be dedicated to one particular field, such as a school of economics or dance. ã€€Alternative schools may provide nontraditional curriculum and methods. ã€€Non-government schools, also known as private schools, may be required when the government does not supply adequate or specific educational needs. ã€€Other private schools can also be religious, such as Christian schools, gurukula (Hindu schools), madrasa (Arabic schools), hawzas (Shi'i Muslim schools), yeshivas (Jewish schools), and others; or schools that have a higher standard of education or seek to foster other personal achievements. ã€€Schools for adults include institutions of corporate training, military education and training and business schools. ã€€Critics of school often accuse the school system of failing to adequately prepare students for their future lives, of encouraging certain temperaments while inhibiting others, of prescribing students exactly what to do, how, when, where and with whom, which would suppress creativity, and of using extrinsic measures such as grades and homework, which would inhibit children's natural curiosity and desire to learn. ã€€In homeschooling and distance education, teaching and learning take place independent from the institution of school or in a virtual school outside a traditional school building, respectively. ã€€Schools are organized in several different organizational models, including departmental, small learning communities, academies, integrated, and schools-within-a-school.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World>World</a> 

<font size=3>
In its most general sense, the term "world" refers to the totality of entities, to the whole of reality or to everything that is. ã€€The nature of the world has been conceptualized differently in different fields. ã€€Some conceptions see the world as unique while others talk of a "plurality of worlds". ã€€Some treat the world as one simple object while others analyze the world as a complex made up of many parts. ã€€In scientific cosmology the world or universe is commonly defined as "[t]he totality of all space and time; all that is, has been, and will be". ã€€Theories of modality, on the other hand, talk of possible worlds as complete and consistent ways how things could have been. ã€€Phenomenology, starting from the horizon of co-given objects present in the periphery of every experience, defines the world as the biggest horizon or the "horizon of all horizons". ã€€In philosophy of mind, the world is commonly contrasted with the mind as that which is represented by the mind. ã€€Theology conceptualizes the world in relation to God, for example, as God's creation, as identical to God or as the two being interdependent. ã€€In religions, there is often a tendency to downgrade the material or sensory world in favor of a spiritual world to be sought through religious practice. ã€€A comprehensive representation of the world and our place in it, as is commonly found in religions, is known as a worldview. ã€€Cosmogony is the field that studies the origin or creation of the world while eschatology refers to the science or doctrine of the last things or of the end of the world. ã€€In various contexts, the term "world" takes a more restricted meaning associated, for example, with the Earth and all life on it, with humanity as a whole or with an international or intercontinental scope. ã€€In this sense, world history refers to the history of humanity as a whole or world politics is the discipline of political science studying issues that transcend nations and continents. ã€€Other examples include terms such as "world religion", "world language", "world government", "world war", "world population", "world economy" or "world championship".
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Year>Year</a> 

<font size=3>
A year is the orbital period of a planetary body, for example, the Earth, moving in its orbit around the Sun. ã€€Due to the Earth's axial tilt, the course of a year sees the passing of the seasons, marked by change in weather, the hours of daylight, and, consequently, vegetation and soil fertility. ã€€In temperate and subpolar regions around the planet, four seasons are generally recognized: spring, summer, autumn and winter. ã€€In tropical and subtropical regions, several geographical sectors do not present defined seasons; but in the seasonal tropics, the annual wet and dry seasons are recognized and tracked. ã€€A calendar year is an approximation of the number of days of the Earth's orbital period, as counted in a given calendar. ã€€The Gregorian calendar, or modern calendar, presents its calendar year to be either a common year of 365 days or a leap year of 366 days, as do the Julian calendars. ã€€For the Gregorian calendar, the average length of the calendar year (the mean year) across the complete leap cycle of 400 years is 365.2425 days (97 out of 400 years are leap years). ã€€In English, the unit of time for year is commonly abbreviated as "y" or "yr". ã€€The symbol "a" is more common in scientific literature, though its exact duration may be inconsistent. ã€€In astronomy, the Julian year is a unit of time defined as 365.25 days of exactly 86,400 seconds (SI base unit), totalling exactly 31,557,600 seconds in the Julian astronomical year. ã€€The word year is also used for periods loosely associated with, but not identical to, the calendar or astronomical year, such as the seasonal year, the fiscal year, the academic year, etc. ã€€Similarly, year can mean the orbital period of any planet; for example, a Martian year and a Venusian year are examples of the time a planet takes to transit one complete orbit. ã€€The term can also be used in reference to any long period or cycle, such as the Great Year.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/People>People</a> 

<font size=3>
A people is any plurality of persons considered as a whole. ã€€Used in politics and law it is a term to refer to the collective or community of an ethnic group, a nation, to the public or common mass of people of a polity. ã€€As such it is a concept of human rights law, international law as well as constitutional law, particularly used for claims of popular sovereignty.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University>University</a> 

<font size=3>
A university (from Latin universitasÂ 'a whole') is an institution of higher (or tertiary) education and research which awards academic degrees in several academic disciplines. ã€€Universities typically offer both undergraduate and postgraduate programs. ã€€In the United States, the designation is reserved for colleges that have a graduate school. ã€€The word university is derived from the Latin universitas magistrorum et scholarium, which roughly means "community of teachers and scholars". ã€€The first universities in Europe were established by Catholic Church monks. ã€€The University of Bologna (UniversitÃ  di Bologna), Italy, which was founded in 1088, is the first university in the sense of: Being a high degree-awarding institute. ã€€Having independence from the ecclesiastic schools, although conducted by both clergy and non-clergy. ã€€Using the word universitas (which was coined at its foundation). ã€€Issuing secular and non-secular degrees: grammar, rhetoric, logic, theology, canon law, notarial law.
</font>

word | phonetic | definition | translation | root | lemma | degre

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/City>City</a> 

<font size=3>
A city is a human settlement of notable size. ã€€It can be defined as a permanent and densely settled place with administratively defined boundaries whose members work primarily on non-agricultural tasks. ã€€Cities generally have extensive systems for housing, transportation, sanitation, utilities, land use, production of goods, and communication. ã€€Their density facilitates interaction between people, government organisations and businesses, sometimes benefiting different parties in the process, such as improving efficiency of goods and service distribution. ã€€Historically, city-dwellers have been a small proportion of humanity overall, but following two centuries of unprecedented and rapid urbanization, more than half of the world population now lives in cities, which has had profound consequences for global sustainability. ã€€Present-day cities usually form the core of larger metropolitan areas and urban areasâ€”creating numerous commuters traveling towards city centres for employment, entertainment, and education. ã€€However, in a world of intensifying globalization, all cities are to varying degrees also connected globally beyond these regions. ã€€This increased influence means that cities also have significant influences on global issues, such as sustainable development, global warming, and global health. ã€€Because of these major influences on global issues, the international community has prioritized investment in sustainable cities through Sustainable Development Goal 11. ã€€Due to the efficiency of transportation and the smaller land consumption, dense cities hold the potential to have a smaller ecological footprint per inhabitant than more sparsely populated areas. ã€€Therefore, compact cities are often referred to as a crucial element of fighting climate change. ã€€However, this concentration can also have significant negative consequences, such as forming urban heat islands, concentrating pollution, and stressing water supplies and other resources. ã€€Other important traits of cities besides population include the capital status and relative continued occupation of the city. ã€€For example, country capitals such as Beijing, London, Mexico City, Moscow, Nairobi, New Delhi, Paris, Rome, Athens, Seoul, Singapore, Tokyo, Manila, and Washington, D.C. reflect the identity and apex of their respective nations. ã€€Some historic capitals, such as Kyoto and Xi'an, maintain their reflection of cultural identity even without modern capital status. ã€€Religious holy sites offer another example of capital status within a religion, Jerusalem, Mecca, Varanasi, Ayodhya, Haridwar and Prayagraj each hold significance.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E>E</a> 

<font size=3>
E, or e, is the fifth letter and the second vowel letter in the Latin alphabet, used in the modern English alphabet, the alphabets of other western European languages and others worldwide. ã€€Its name in English is e (pronounced /ËˆiË/); plural ees, Es or E's. ã€€It is the most commonly used letter in many languages, including Czech, Danish, Dutch, English, French, German, Hungarian, Latin, Latvian, Norwegian, Spanish, and Swedish.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second>Second</a> 

<font size=3>
The second (symbol: s) is the unit of time in the International System of Units (SI), historically defined as 1â„86400 of a day â€“ this factor derived from the division of the day first into 24 hours, then to 60 minutes and finally to 60 seconds each (24 Ã— 60 Ã— 60 = 86400). ã€€The current and formal definition in the International System of Units (SI) is more precise: The second [...] is defined by taking the fixed numerical value of the caesium frequency, Î”Î½Cs, the unperturbed ground-state hyperfine transition frequency of the caesium 133 atom, to be 9192631770 when expressed in the unit Hz, which is equal to sâˆ’1. ã€€This current definition was adopted in 1967 when it became feasible to define the second based on fundamental properties of nature with caesium clocks. ã€€Because the speed of Earth's rotation varies and is slowing ever so slightly, a leap second is added at irregular intervals to civil time to keep clocks in sync with Earth's rotation.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Day>Day</a> 

<font size=3>
A day is the time period of a full rotation of the Earth with respect to the Sun. ã€€On average, this is 24 hours, 1440 minutes, or 86,400 seconds. ã€€In everyday life, the word "day" often refers to a solar day, which is the length between two solar noons or times the Sun reaches the highest point. ã€€The word "day" may also refer to daytime, a time period when the location receives direct and indirect sunlight. ã€€On Earth, as a location passes through its day, it experiences morning, noon, afternoon, evening, and night. ã€€The effect of a day is vital to many life processes, which is called the circadian rhythm. ã€€A collection of sequential days is organized into calendars as dates, almost always into weeks, months and years. ã€€Most calendars' arrangement of dates use either or both the Sun with its four seasons (solar calendar) or the Moon's phasing (lunar calendar). ã€€The start of a day is commonly accepted as roughly the time of the middle of the night or midnight, written as 00:00 or 12:00 am in 24- or 12-hour clocks, respectively. ã€€Because the time of midnight varies between locations, time zones are set up to facilitate the use of a uniform standard time. ã€€Midnight is not the only convention used to determine the start of a new day. ã€€Other defining moments have been used throughout history, and some are used even today, such as with the Jewish religious calendar, which counts days from sunset to sunset, so the Jewish Sabbath begins at sundown on Friday. ã€€Astronomers also have a convention where their day begins at high noon. ã€€This way, all of their observations throughout a single night are recorded as happening on the same day. ã€€This method removes ambiguity of a particular observation happening on a calendar day, eliminating the need to further determine which night it happened on. ã€€Because when using midnight as the start of day, each calendar day is associated with two separate night periods. ã€€In specific applications, the definition of a day is slightly modified, such as in the ISQ day (exactly 86,400 seconds) used for computers and standards keeping, local mean time accounting of the Earth's natural fluctuation of a solar day, and stellar day and sidereal day (using the celestial sphere) used for astronomy. ã€€In most countries outside of the tropics, daylight saving time is practiced, and each year there will be one 23-hour civil day and one 25-hour civil day. ã€€Due to slight variations in the rotation of the Earth, there are rare times when a leap second will get inserted at the end of a UTC day, and so while almost all days have a duration of 86,400 seconds, there are these exceptional cases of a day with 86,401 seconds (in the half-century spanning 1972 through 2022, there have been a total of 27 leap seconds that have been inserted, so roughly once every other year).
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Film>Film</a> 

<font size=3>
A film â€“ also called a movie, motion picture, moving picture, picture, photoplay or (slang) flick â€“ is a work of visual art that simulates experiences and otherwise communicates ideas, stories, perceptions, feelings, beauty, or atmosphere through the use of moving images. ã€€These images are generally accompanied by sound and, more rarely, other sensory stimulations. ã€€The word "cinema", short for cinematography, is often used to refer to filmmaking and the film industry, and to the art form that is the result of it.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Still>Still</a> 

<font size=3>
A still is an apparatus used to distill liquid mixtures by heating to selectively boil and then cooling to condense the vapor. ã€€It still uses the same concepts as a basic distillation apparatus, but on a much larger scale. ã€€Stills have been used to produce perfume and medicine, water for injection (WFI) for pharmaceutical use, generally to separate and purify different chemicals, and to produce distilled beverages containing ethanol.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Head>Head</a> 

<font size=3>
A head is the part of an organism which usually includes the ears, brain, forehead, cheeks, chin, eyes, nose, and mouth, each of which aid in various sensory functions such as sight, hearing, smell, and taste. ã€€Some very simple animals may not have a head, but many bilaterally symmetric forms do, regardless of size. ã€€Heads develop in animals by an evolutionary trend known as cephalization. ã€€In bilaterally symmetrical animals, nervous tissue concentrate at the anterior region, forming structures responsible for information processing. ã€€Through biological evolution, sense organs and feeding structures also concentrate into the anterior region; these collectively form the head.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Head_(hydrology)>Head (hydrology)</a> 

<font size=3>
In hydrology, the head is the point on a watercourse up to which it has been artificially broadened and/or raised by an impoundment. ã€€Above the head of the reservoir natural conditions prevail; below it the water level above the riverbed has been raised by the impoundment and its flow rate reduced, unless and until banks, barrages, weir sluices or dams are overcome (overtopped), whereby a less frictional than natural course will exist (mid-level and surface rather than bed and bank currents) resulting in flash flooding below. ã€€In principle, a distinction must be drawn between the head of a reservoir impounded by a dam, and the head of a works resulting from a barrage or canal locks.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Head_(geology)>Head (geology)</a> 

<font size=3>
Head describes deposits consisting of fragmented material which, following weathering, have moved downslope through a process of solifluction. ã€€The term has been used by British geologists since the middle of the 19th century to describe such material in a range of different settings from flat hilltops to the bottoms of valleys. ã€€Areas identified as head include deposits of aeolian origin such as blown sand and loess, slope deposits such as gelifluctates and solifluctates, and recently eroded soil material, called colluvium. ã€€With geologists becoming more interested in studying the near-surface environment and its related processes, the term head is becoming obsolete. ã€€A related term is 'combe (or coombe) rock', descriptive of a body of chalk and flint fragments contained within a mass of chalky earth typically found on the chalk downlands of south-east England and resulting from freeze-thaw processes. ã€€Where the mass is also soliflucted, it is considered a variety of head. ã€€Though its earliest use is attributed to De la Beche in 1839 he mentions that in 1837 Mr. Trevelyn of Guernsey observed â€œ a bed of disintegrated granite, about three feet thick, mixed with angular fragments, thus reminding us of the head of angular fragments so commonly seen in Cornwall and Devon.ã€€â€œ
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Man>Man</a> 

<font size=3>
A man is an adult male human. ã€€Prior to adulthood, a male human is referred to as a boy (a male child or adolescent). ã€€Like most other male mammals, a man's genome usually inherits an XÂ chromosome from the mother and a YÂ chromosome from the father. ã€€Sex differentiation of the male fetus is governed by the SRY gene on the Y chromosome. ã€€During puberty, hormones which stimulate androgen production result in the development of secondary sexual characteristics, thus exhibiting greater differences between the sexes. ã€€These include greater muscle mass, the growth of facial hair and a lower body fat composition. ã€€Male anatomy is distinguished from female anatomy by the male reproductive system, which includes the penis, testicles, sperm duct, prostate gland and the epididymis, and by secondary sex characteristics, including a narrower pelvis, narrower hips, and smaller breasts without mammary glands. ã€€Throughout human history, traditional gender roles have often defined and limited men's activities and opportunities. ã€€Men often face conscription into military service or are directed into professions with high mortality rates, resulting in a shorter life expectancy than women. ã€€Many religious doctrines stipulate certain rules for men, such as forced circumcision. ã€€Men are over-represented as both perpetrators and victims of violence. ã€€Trans men have a gender identity that does not align with their female sex assignment at birth, while intersex men may have sex characteristics that do not fit typical notions of male biology.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Team>Team</a> 

<font size=3>
A team is a group of individuals (human or non-human) working together to achieve their goal. ã€€As defined by Professor Leigh Thompson of the Kellogg School of Management, "[a] team is a group of people who are interdependent with respect to information, resources, knowledge and skills and who seek to combine their efforts to achieve a common goal". ã€€A group does not necessarily constitute a team. ã€€Teams normally have members with complementary skills and generate synergy through a coordinated effort which allows each member to maximize their strengths and minimize their weaknesses. ã€€Naresh Jain (2009) claims: Team members need to learn how to help one another, help other team members realize their true potential, and create an environment that allows everyone to go beyond their limitations. ã€€While academic research on teams and teamwork has grown consistently and has shown a sharp increase over the past recent 40 years, the societal diffusion of teams and teamwork actually followed a volatile trend in the 20th century. ã€€The concept was introduced into business in the late 20th century, which was followed by a popularization of the concept of constructing teams. ã€€Differing opinions exist on the efficacy of this new management fad. ã€€Some see "team" as a four-letter word: overused and under-useful. ã€€Others see it as a panacea that realizes the Human Relations Movement's desire to integrate what that movement perceives as best for workers and as best for managers. ã€€Many people believe in the effectiveness of teams, but also see them as dangerous because of the potential for exploiting workers â€” in that team effectiveness can rely on peer pressure and peer surveillance. ã€€However, Hackman sees team effectiveness not only in terms of performance: a truly effective team will contribute to the personal well-being and adaptive growth of its members. ã€€English-speakers commonly use the word "team" in today's society to characterise many types of groups. ã€€Peter Guy Northouse's book Leadership: theory and practice discusses teams from a leadership perspective. ã€€According to the team approach to leadership, a team is a type of organizational group of people that are members.ã€€[citation needed] A team is composed of members who are dependent on each other, work towards interchangeable achievements, and share common attainments. ã€€A team works as a whole together to achieve certain things. ã€€A team is usually located in the same setting as it is normally connected to a kind of organization, company, or community. ã€€Teams can meet in-person (directly face-to-face) or virtually when practicing their values and activities or duties. ã€€A team's communication is significantly important to their relationship.ã€€[citation needed] Ergo, communication is frequent and persistent, and as well are the meetings.ã€€[citation needed] The definition of team as an organizational group is not completely set in stone, as organizations have confronted a myriad[quantify] of new forms of contemporary collaboration. ã€€Teams usually have strong organizational structured platforms and respond quickly and efficiently to challenges as they have skills and the capability to do so.ã€€[citation needed] An effective organizational team leads to greater productivity, more effective implementation of resources, better decisions and problem-solving, better-quality products/service, and greater innovation and originality.ã€€[citation needed] Alongside the concept of a team, compare the more structured/skilled concept of a crew, the advantages of formal and informal partnerships, or the well-defined â€“ but time-limited â€“ existence of task forces. ã€€A team becomes more than just a collection of people when a strong sense of mutual commitment creates synergy, thus generating performance greater than the sum of the performance of its individual members. ã€€Thus teams of game players can form (and re-form) to practise their craft/sport. ã€€Transport logistics executives can select teams of horses, dogs, or oxen for the purpose of conveying passengers or goods.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family>Family</a> 

<font size=3>
Family (from Latin: familia) is a group of people related either by consanguinity (by recognized birth) or affinity (by marriage or other relationship). ã€€The purpose of the family is to maintain the well-being of its members and of society. ã€€Ideally, families offer predictability, structure, and safety as members mature and learn to participate in the community. ã€€Historically, most human societies use family as the primary locus of attachment, nurturance, and socialization. ã€€Anthropologists classify most family organizations as matrifocal (a mother and her children), patrifocal (a father and his children), conjugal (a wife, her husband, and children, also called the nuclear family), avuncular (a man, his sister, and her children), or extended (in addition to parents and children, may include grandparents, aunts, uncles, or cousins). ã€€The field of genealogy aims to trace family lineages through history. ã€€The family is also an important economic unit studied in family economics. ã€€The word "families" can be used metaphorically to create more inclusive categories such as community, nationhood, and global village.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)>Family (biology)</a> 

<font size=3>
Family (Latin: familia, plural familiae) is one of the eight major hierarchical taxonomic ranks in Linnaean taxonomy. ã€€It is classified between order and genus. ã€€A family may be divided into subfamilies, which are intermediate ranks between the ranks of family and genus. ã€€The official family names are Latin in origin; however, popular names are often used: for example, walnut trees and hickory trees belong to the family Juglandaceae, but that family is commonly referred to as the "walnut family". ã€€What belongs to a familyâ€”or if a described family should be recognized at allâ€”are proposed and determined by practicing taxonomists. ã€€There are no hard rules for describing or recognizing a family, but in plants, they can be characterized on the basis of both vegetative and reproductive features of plant species. ã€€Taxonomists often take different positions about descriptions, and there may be no broad consensus across the scientific community for some time. ã€€The publishing of new data and opinions often enables adjustments and consensus.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House>House</a> 

<font size=3>
A house is a single-unit residential building. ã€€It may range in complexity from a rudimentary hut to a complex structure of wood, masonry, concrete or other material, outfitted with plumbing, electrical, and heating, ventilation, and air conditioning systems. ã€€Houses use a range of different roofing systems to keep precipitation such as rain from getting into the dwelling space. ã€€Houses may have doors or locks to secure the dwelling space and protect its inhabitants and contents from burglars or other trespassers. ã€€Most conventional modern houses in Western cultures will contain one or more bedrooms and bathrooms, a kitchen or cooking area, and a living room. ã€€A house may have a separate dining room, or the eating area may be integrated into another room. ã€€Some large houses in North America have a recreation room. ã€€In traditional agriculture-oriented societies, domestic animals such as chickens or larger livestock (like cattle) may share part of the house with humans. ã€€The social unit that lives in a house is known as a household. ã€€Most commonly, a household is a family unit of some kind, although households may also be other social groups, such as roommates or, in a rooming house, unconnected individuals. ã€€Some houses only have a dwelling space for one family or similar-sized group; larger houses called townhouses or row houses may contain numerous family dwellings in the same structure. ã€€A house may be accompanied by outbuildings, such as a garage for vehicles or a shed for gardening equipment and tools. ã€€A house may have a backyard or a front yard or both, which serve as additional areas where inhabitants can relax or eat.
</font>

Most horoscopic traditions of astrology systems divide the horoscope into a number (usually twelve) of houses whose positions depend on time and location rather than on date. ã€€In Hindu astrological tradition these are known as BhÄvas. ã€€The houses of the horoscope represent different fields of experience wherein the energies of the signs and planets operateâ€”described in terms of physical surroundings as well as personal life experiences.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War>War</a> 

<font size=3>
War is an intense armed conflict between states, governments, societies, or paramilitary groups such as mercenaries, insurgents, and militias. ã€€It is generally characterized by extreme violence, destruction, and mortality, using regular or irregular military forces. ã€€Warfare refers to the common activities and characteristics of types of war, or of wars in general. ã€€Total war is warfare that is not restricted to purely legitimate military targets, and can result in massive civilian or other non-combatant suffering and casualties. ã€€While some war studies scholars consider war a universal and ancestral aspect of human nature, others argue it is a result of specific socio-cultural, economic or ecological circumstances.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life>Life</a> 

<font size=3>
Life is a quality that distinguishes matter that has biological processes, such as signaling and self-sustaining processes, from matter that does not, and is defined by the capacity for growth, reaction to stimuli, metabolism, energy transformation, and reproduction. ã€€Various forms of life exist, such as plants, animals, fungi, protists, archaea, and bacteria. ã€€Biology is the science that studies life. ã€€The gene is the unit of heredity, whereas the cell is the structural and functional unit of life. ã€€There are two kinds of cells, prokaryotic and eukaryotic, both of which consist of cytoplasm enclosed within a membrane and contain many biomolecules such as proteins and nucleic acids. ã€€Cells reproduce through a process of cell division, in which the parent cell divides into two or more daughter cells and passes its genes onto a new generation, sometimes producing genetic variation. ã€€Organisms, or the individual entities of life, are generally thought to be open systems that maintain homeostasis, are composed of cells, have a life cycle, undergo metabolism, can grow, adapt to their environment, respond to stimuli, reproduce and evolve over multiple generations. ã€€Other definitions sometimes include non-cellular life forms such as viruses and viroids, but they are usually excluded because they do not function on their own; rather, they exploit the biological processes of hosts. ã€€Abiogenesis, also known as the origin of life, is the natural process of life arising from non-living matter, such as simple organic compounds. ã€€Since its primordial beginnings, life on Earth has changed its environment on a geologic time scale, but it has also adapted to survive in most ecosystems and conditions. ã€€New lifeforms have evolved from common ancestors through hereditary variation and natural selection, and today, estimates of the number of distinct species range anywhere from 3 million to over 100Â million. ã€€Death is the permanent termination of all biological processes which sustain an organism, and as such, is the end of its life. ã€€Extinction is the term describing the dying-out of a group or taxon, usually a species. ã€€Once extinct, the extinct species or taxon cannot come back to life. ã€€Fossils are the preserved remains or traces of organisms.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Name>Name</a> 

<font size=3>
A name is a term used for identification by an external observer. ã€€They can identify a class or category of things, or a single thing, either uniquely, or within a given context. ã€€The entity identified by a name is called its referent. ã€€A personal name identifies, not necessarily uniquely, a specific individual human. ã€€The name of a specific entity is sometimes called a proper name (although that term has a philosophical meaning as well) and is, when consisting of only one word, a proper noun. ã€€Other nouns are sometimes called "common names" or (obsolete) "general names". ã€€A name can be given to a person, place, or thing; for example, parents can give their child a name or a scientist can give an element a name.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number>Number</a> 

<font size=3>
A number is a mathematical object used to count, measure, and label. ã€€The original examples are the natural numbers 1, 2, 3, 4, and so forth. ã€€Numbers can be represented in language with number words. ã€€More universally, individual numbers can be represented by symbols, called numerals; for example, "5" is a numeral that represents the number five. ã€€As only a relatively small number of symbols can be memorized, basic numerals are commonly organized in a numeral system, which is an organized way to represent any number. ã€€The most common numeral system is the Hinduâ€“Arabic numeral system, which allows for the representation of any number using a combination of ten fundamental numeric symbols, called digits. ã€€In addition to their use in counting and measuring, numerals are often used for labels (as with telephone numbers), for ordering (as with serial numbers), and for codes (as with ISBNs). ã€€In common usage, a numeral is not clearly distinguished from the number that it represents. ã€€In mathematics, the notion of a number has been extended over the centuries to include zero (0), negative numbers, rational numbers such as one half ( 1 2 ) {\displaystyle \left({\tfrac {1}{2}}\right)} , real numbers such as the square root of 2 ( 2 ) {\displaystyle \left({\sqrt {2}}\right)} and Ï€, and complex numbers which extend the real numbers with a square root of âˆ’1 (and its combinations with real numbers by adding or subtracting its multiples). ã€€Calculations with numbers are done with arithmetical operations, the most familiar being addition, subtraction, multiplication, division, and exponentiation. ã€€Their study or usage is called arithmetic, a term which may also refer to number theory, the study of the properties of numbers. ã€€Besides their practical uses, numbers have cultural significance throughout the world. ã€€For example, in Western society, the number 13 is often regarded as unlucky, and "a million" may signify "a lot" rather than an exact quantity. ã€€Though it is now regarded as pseudoscience, belief in a mystical significance of numbers, known as numerology, permeated ancient and medieval thought. ã€€Numerology heavily influenced the development of Greek mathematics, stimulating the investigation of many problems in number theory which are still of interest today. ã€€During the 19th century, mathematicians began to develop many different abstractions which share certain properties of numbers, and may be seen as extending the concept. ã€€Among the first were the hypercomplex numbers, which consist of various extensions or modifications of the complex number system. ã€€In modern mathematics, number systems are considered important special examples of more general algebraic structures such as rings and fields, and the application of the term "number" is a matter of convention, without fundamental significance.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R>R</a> 

<font size=3>
R, or r, is the eighteenth letter of the Latin alphabet, used in the modern English alphabet, the alphabets of other western European languages and others worldwide. ã€€Its name in English is ar (pronounced /ËˆÉ‘Ër/), plural ars, or in Ireland or /ËˆÉ”Ër/. ã€€The letter âŸ¨râŸ© is the eighth most common letter in English and the fourth-most common consonant (after âŸ¨tâŸ©, âŸ¨nâŸ©, and âŸ¨sâŸ©). ã€€The letter âŸ¨râŸ© is used to form the ending "-re", which is used in certain words such as centre in some varieties of English spelling, such as British English. ã€€Canadian English also uses the "-re" ending, unlike American English, where the ending is usually replaced by "-er" (center). ã€€This does not affect pronunciation.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Following>Following</a> 

<font size=3>
Following is a 1998 independent neo-noir crime thriller film written, produced, edited and directed by Christopher Nolan. ã€€It tells the story of a young man who follows strangers around the streets of London and is drawn into a criminal underworld when he fails to keep his distance. ã€€As Nolan's debut feature, it was designed to be as inexpensive as possible to make. ã€€Scenes were heavily rehearsed so that just one or two takes were needed to economise on 16mm film stock, the production's greatest expense, and for which Nolan was paying from his salary. ã€€Unable to afford expensive professional lighting equipment, Nolan mostly used available light. ã€€Along with writing, directing, and photographing the film, Nolan also helped in editing and production. ã€€The film was released by The Criterion Collection on both Blu-ray and DVD in North America on 11 December 2012.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Season>Season</a> 

<font size=3>
A season is a division of the year based on changes in weather, ecology, and the number of daylight hours in a given region. ã€€On Earth, seasons are the result of the axial parallelism of Earth's tilted orbit around the Sun. ã€€In temperate and polar regions, the seasons are marked by changes in the intensity of sunlight that reaches the Earth's surface, variations of which may cause animals to undergo hibernation or to migrate, and plants to be dormant. ã€€Various cultures define the number and nature of seasons based on regional variations, and as such there are a number of both modern and historical cultures whose number of seasons varies. ã€€The Northern Hemisphere experiences most direct sunlight during May, June, and July, as the hemisphere faces the Sun. ã€€The same is true of the Southern Hemisphere in November, December, and January. ã€€It is Earth's axial tilt that causes the Sun to be higher in the sky during the summer months, which increases the solar flux. ã€€However, due to seasonal lag, June, July, and August are the warmest months in the Northern Hemisphere while December, January, and February are the warmest months in the Southern Hemisphere. ã€€In temperate and sub-polar regions, four seasons based on the Gregorian calendar are generally recognized: spring, summer, autumn (or fall), and winter. ã€€Ecologists often use a six-season model for temperate climate regions which are not tied to any fixed calendar dates: prevernal, vernal, estival, serotinal, autumnal, and hibernal. ã€€Many tropical regions have two seasons: the rainy, wet, or monsoon season and the dry season. ã€€Some have a third cool, mild, or harmattan season. ã€€"Seasons" can also be dictated by the timing of important ecological events such as hurricane season, tornado season, and wildfire season.ã€€[citation needed] Some examples of historical importance are the ancient Egyptian seasonsâ€”flood, growth, and low waterâ€”which were previously defined by the former annual flooding of the Nile in Egypt. ã€€Seasons often hold special significance for agrarian societies, whose lives revolve around planting and harvest times, and the change of seasons is often attended by ritual. ã€€The definition of seasons is also cultural. ã€€In India, from ancient times to the present day, six seasons or Ritu based on south Asian religious or cultural calendars are recognised and identified for purposes such as agriculture and trade.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government>Government</a> 

<font size=3>
A government is the system or group of people governing an organized community, generally a state. ã€€In the case of its broad associative definition, government normally consists of legislature, executive, and judiciary. ã€€Government is a means by which organizational policies are enforced, as well as a mechanism for determining policy. ã€€In many countries, the government has a kind of constitution, a statement of its governing principles and philosophy. ã€€While all types of organizations have governance, the term government is often used more specifically to refer to the approximately 200 independent national governments and subsidiary organizations. ã€€The major types of political systems in the modern era are democracies, monarchies, authoritarian and totalitarian regimes. ã€€Historically prevalent forms of government include monarchy, aristocracy, timocracy, oligarchy, democracy, theocracy, and tyranny. ã€€These forms are not always mutually exclusive, and mixed governments are common. ã€€The main aspect of any philosophy of government is how political power is obtained, with the two main forms being electoral contest and hereditary succession.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/System>System</a> 

<font size=3>
A system is a group of interacting or interrelated elements that act according to a set of rules to form a unified whole. ã€€A system, surrounded and influenced by its environment, is described by its boundaries, structure and purpose and expressed in its functioning. ã€€Systems are the subjects of study of systems theory and other systems sciences. ã€€Systems have several common properties and characteristics, including structure, function(s), behavior and interconnectivity.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Company>Company</a> 

<font size=3>
A company, abbreviated as co., is a legal entity representing an association of people, whether natural, legal or a mixture of both, with a specific objective. ã€€Company members share a common purpose and unite to achieve specific, declared goals. ã€€Companies take various forms, such as: voluntary associations, which may include nonprofit organizations business entities, whose aim is generating profit financial entities and banks programs or educational institutions A company can be created as a legal person so that the company itself has limited liability as members perform or fail to discharge their duty according to the publicly declared incorporation, or published policy. ã€€When a company closes, it may need to be liquidated to avoid further legal obligations. ã€€Companies may associate and collectively register themselves as new companies; the resulting entities are often known as corporate groups.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/End_(topology)>End (topology)</a> 

<font size=3>
In topology, a branch of mathematics, the ends of a topological space are, roughly speaking, the connected components of the "ideal boundary" of the space. ã€€That is, each end represents a topologically distinct way to move to infinity within the space. ã€€Adding a point at each end yields a compactification of the original space, known as the end compactification. ã€€The notion of an end of a topological space was introduced by Hans FreudenthalÂ (1931).
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Area>Area</a> 

<font size=3>
Area is the measure of a region's size on a surface. ã€€The area of a plane region or plane area refers to the area of a shape or planar lamina, while surface area refers to the area of an open surface or the boundary of a three-dimensional object. ã€€Area can be understood as the amount of material with a given thickness that would be necessary to fashion a model of the shape, or the amount of paint necessary to cover the surface with a single coat. ã€€It is the two-dimensional analogue of the length of a curve (a one-dimensional concept) or the volume of a solid (a three-dimensional concept). ã€€The area of a shape can be measured by comparing the shape to squares of a fixed size. ã€€In the International System of Units (SI), the standard unit of area is the square metre (written as m2), which is the area of a square whose sides are one metre long. ã€€A shape with an area of three square metres would have the same area as three such squares. ã€€In mathematics, the unit square is defined to have area one, and the area of any other shape or surface is a dimensionless real number. ã€€There are several well-known formulas for the areas of simple shapes such as triangles, rectangles, and circles. ã€€Using these formulas, the area of any polygon can be found by dividing the polygon into triangles. ã€€For shapes with curved boundary, calculus is usually required to compute the area. ã€€Indeed, the problem of determining the area of plane figures was a major motivation for the historical development of calculus. ã€€For a solid shape such as a sphere, cone, or cylinder, the area of its boundary surface is called the surface area. ã€€Formulas for the surface areas of simple shapes were computed by the ancient Greeks, but computing the surface area of a more complicated shape usually requires multivariable calculus. ã€€Area plays an important role in modern mathematics. ã€€In addition to its obvious importance in geometry and calculus, area is related to the definition of determinants in linear algebra, and is a basic property of surfaces in differential geometry. ã€€In analysis, the area of a subset of the plane is defined using Lebesgue measure, though not every subset is measurable. ã€€In general, area in higher mathematics is seen as a special case of volume for two-dimensional regions. ã€€Area can be defined through the use of axioms, defining it as a function of a collection of certain plane figures to the set of real numbers. ã€€It can be proved that such a function exists.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand>Hand</a> 

<font size=3>
A hand is a prehensile, multi-fingered appendage located at the end of the forearm or forelimb of primates such as humans, chimpanzees, monkeys, and lemurs. ã€€A few other vertebrates such as the koala (which has two opposable thumbs on each "hand" and fingerprints extremely similar to human fingerprints) are often described as having "hands" instead of paws on their front limbs. ã€€The raccoon is usually described as having "hands" though opposable thumbs are lacking. ã€€Some evolutionary anatomists use the term hand to refer to the appendage of digits on the forelimb more generallyâ€”for example, in the context of whether the three digits of the bird hand involved the same homologous loss of two digits as in the dinosaur hand. ã€€The human hand usually has five digits: four fingers plus one thumb; these are often referred to collectively as five fingers, however, whereby the thumb is included as one of the fingers. ã€€It has 27 bones, not including the sesamoid bone, the number of which varies among people, 14 of which are the phalanges (proximal, intermediate and distal) of the fingers and thumb. ã€€The metacarpal bones connect the fingers and the carpal bones of the wrist. ã€€Each human hand has five metacarpals and eight carpal bones. ã€€Fingers contain some of the densest areas of nerve endings in the body, and are the richest source of tactile feedback. ã€€They also have the greatest positioning capability of the body; thus, the sense of touch is intimately associated with hands. ã€€Like other paired organs (eyes, feet, legs) each hand is dominantly controlled by the opposing brain hemisphere, so that handednessâ€”the preferred hand choice for single-handed activities such as writing with a pencil, reflects individual brain functioning. ã€€Among humans, the hands play an important function in body language and sign language. ã€€Likewise, the ten digits of two hands and the twelve phalanges of four fingers (touchable by the thumb) have given rise to number systems and calculation techniques.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Home>Home</a> 

<font size=3>
A home, or domicile, is a space used as a permanent or semi-permanent residence for one or many humans, and sometimes various companion animals. ã€€It is a fully or semi sheltered space and can have both interior and exterior aspects to it. ã€€Homes provide sheltered spaces, for instance rooms, where domestic activity can be performed such as sleeping, preparing food, eating and hygiene as well as providing spaces for work and leisure such as remote working, studying and playing. ã€€Physical forms of homes can be static such as a house or an apartment, mobile such as a houseboat, trailer or yurt or digital such as virtual space. ã€€The aspect of â€˜homeâ€™ can be considered across scales; from the micro scale showcasing the most intimate spaces of the individual dwelling and direct surrounding area to the macro scale of the geographic area such as town, village, city, country or planet. ã€€The concept of â€˜homeâ€™ has been researched and theorized across disciplines â€“ topics ranging from the idea of home, the interior, the psyche, liminal space, contested space to gender and politics. ã€€The home as a concept expands beyond residence as contemporary lifestyles and technological advances redefine the way the global population lives and works.ã€€[citation needed] The concept and experience encompasses the likes of exile, yearning, belonging, homesickness and homelessness.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_(biology)>Order (biology)</a> 

<font size=3>
Order (Latin: ordo) is one of the eight major hierarchical taxonomic ranks in Linnaean taxonomy. ã€€It is classified between family and class. ã€€In biological classification, the order is a taxonomic rank used in the classification of organisms and recognized by the nomenclature codes. ã€€An immediately higher rank, superorder, is sometimes added directly above order, with suborder directly beneath order. ã€€An order can also be defined as a group of related families. ã€€What does and does not belong to each order is determined by a taxonomist, as is whether a particular order should be recognized at all. ã€€Often there is no exact agreement, with different taxonomists each taking a different position. ã€€There are no hard rules that a taxonomist needs to follow in describing or recognizing an order. ã€€Some taxa are accepted almost universally, while others are recognized only rarely. ã€€The name of an order is usually written with a capital letter. ã€€For some groups of organisms, their orders may follow consistent naming schemes. ã€€Orders of plants, fungi, and algae use the suffix -ales (e.g. Dictyotales). ã€€Orders of birds and fishes use the Latin suffix -(i)formes meaning 'having the form of' (e.g. Passeriformes), but orders of mammals and invertebrates are not so consistent (e.g. Artiodactyla, Actiniaria, Primates).
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Music>Music</a> 

<font size=3>
Music is generally defined as the art of arranging sound to create some combination of form, harmony, melody, rhythm or otherwise expressive content. ã€€Exact definitions of music vary considerably around the world, though it is an aspect of all human societies, a cultural universal. ã€€While scholars agree that music is defined by a few specific elements, there is no consensus on their precise definitions. ã€€The creation of music is commonly divided into musical composition, musical improvisation, and musical performance, though the topic itself extends into academic disciplines, criticism, philosophy, and psychology. ã€€Music may be performed or improvised using a vast range of instruments, including the human voice. ã€€In some musical contexts, a performance or composition may be to some extent improvised. ã€€For instance, in Hindustani classical music, the performer plays spontaneously while following a partially defined structure and using characteristic motifs. ã€€In modal jazz the performers may take turns leading and responding, while sharing a changing set of notes. ã€€In a free jazz context, there may be no structure whatsoever, with each performer acting at their discretion. ã€€Music may be deliberately composed to be unperformable, or agglomerated electronically from many performances. ã€€Music is played in public and private areas, highlighted at events such as festivals, rock concerts, and orchestra performance, and heard incidentally as part of a score or soundtrack to a film, TV show, opera, or video game. ã€€Musical playback is the primary function of an MP3 player or CD player and a universal feature of radios and smartphones. ã€€Music often plays a key role in social activities, religious rituals, rite of passage ceremonies, celebrations, and cultural activities. ã€€The music industry includes songwriters, performers, sound engineers, producers, tour organizers, distributors of instruments, accessories, and sheet music. ã€€Compositions, performances, and recordings are assessed and evaluated by music critics, music journalists, and music scholars, as well as amateurs.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(psychology)>Set (psychology)</a> 

<font size=3>
In psychology, a set is a group of expectations that shape experience by making people especially sensitive to specific kinds of information. ã€€A perceptual set, also called perceptual expectancy, is a predisposition to perceive things in a certain way. ã€€Perceptual sets occur in all the different senses. ã€€They can be long term, such as a special sensitivity to hearing one's own name in a crowded room, or short term, as in the ease with which hungry people notice the smell of food. ã€€A mental set is a framework for thinking about a problem. ã€€It can be shaped by habit or by desire. ã€€Mental sets can make it easy to solve a class of problem, but attachment to the wrong mental set can inhibit problem-solving and creativity.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Game>Game</a> 

<font size=3>
A game is a structured form of play, usually undertaken for entertainment or fun, and sometimes used as an educational tool. ã€€Many games are also considered to be work (such as professional players of spectator sports or games) or art (such as jigsaw puzzles or games involving an artistic layout such as Mahjong, solitaire, or some video games). ã€€Games are sometimes played purely for enjoyment, sometimes for achievement or reward as well. ã€€They can be played alone, in teams, or online; by amateurs or by professionals. ã€€The players may have an audience of non-players, such as when people are entertained by watching a chess championship. ã€€On the other hand, players in a game may constitute their own audience as they take their turn to play. ã€€Often, part of the entertainment for children playing a game is deciding who is part of their audience and who is a player. ã€€A toy and a game are not the same. ã€€Toys generally allow for unrestricted play whereas games come with present rules. ã€€Key components of games are goals, rules, challenge, and interaction[disambiguation needed]. ã€€Games generally involve mental or physical stimulation, and often both. ã€€Many games help develop practical skills, serve as a form of exercise, or otherwise perform an educational, simulational, or psychological role. ã€€Attested as early as 2600 BC, games are a universal part of human experience and present in all cultures. ã€€The Royal Game of Ur, Senet, and Mancala are some of the oldest known games.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Side_(mythology)>Side (mythology)</a> 

<font size=3>
In Greek mythology, Side (Ancient Greek: Î£Î¯Î´Î· 'pomegranate) or Sida was the name of the following figures: Sida, eponym of the city of Sidon in Phoenicia. ã€€She was the wife of Belus, king of Egypt and mother of Aegyptus and Danaus. ã€€Otherwise, the wife of Belus was called Achiroe, daughter of the river-god Nilus. ã€€Side, one of the DanaÃ¯des, condemned to Tartarus for murdering her husband. ã€€From her, a town in Laconia was believed to derived its name from. ã€€Side, the first wife of Orion and mother of his daughters Metioche and Menippe. ã€€She was cast by Hera into Hades because she rivaled the goddess in beauty. ã€€Modern scholars interpret the supposed marriage of Orion to Side ('pomegranate') as a mythical expression for the ripening of the fruit in the season when the constellation Orion is visible in the night sky. ã€€Side, a mortal woman who was chased down by her father Ictinus, intending to rape her. ã€€Side killed herself on her mother's grave, and the gods turned her blood into a pomegranate tree. ã€€Her father was changed into a kite bird that never rested on pomegranate trees.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public>Public</a> 

<font size=3>
In public relations and communication science, publics are groups of individual people, and the public (a.k.a. the general public) is the totality of such groupings. ã€€This is a different concept to the sociological concept of the Ã–ffentlichkeit or public sphere. ã€€The concept of a public has also been defined in political science, psychology, marketing, and advertising. ã€€In public relations and communication science, it is one of the more ambiguous concepts in the field. ã€€Although it has definitions in the theory of the field that have been formulated from the early 20th century onwards, and suffered more recent years from being blurred, as a result of conflation of the idea of a public with the notions of audience, market segment, community, constituency, and stakeholder.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Came>Came</a> 

<font size=3>
A came is a divider bar used between small pieces of glass to make a larger glazing panel. ã€€There are two kinds of came: the H-shaped sections that hold two pieces together and the U-shaped sections that are used for the borders. ã€€Cames are mostly made of lead, zinc, copper, brass or brass-capped lead. ã€€Of the metal strips, lead is softer and more flexible, making it easier to cut and bend. ã€€The harder metals are used to work with slightly curved lines and pieces that require greater structural support. ã€€They can also be used as border came, once again for stability and support.
</font>
