## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/County>County</a> 

<font size=3>
A county is a geographic region of a country used for administrative or other purposes in certain modern nations. ã€€The term is derived from the Old French comtÃ© denoting a jurisdiction under the sovereignty of a count (earl) or a viscount. ã€€Literal equivalents in other languages, derived from the equivalent of "count", are now seldom used officially, including comtÃ©, contea, contado, comtat, condado, Grafschaft, graafschap, and zhupa in Slavic languages; terms equivalent to commune/community are now often instead used. ã€€When the Normans conquered England, they brought the term with them. ã€€The Saxons had already established the districts that became the historic counties of England, calling them shires; many county names derive from the name of the county town (county seat) with the word shire added on: for example, Gloucestershire and Worcestershire. ã€€The Anglo-Saxon terms earl and earldom were taken as equivalent to the continental terms "count" and "county" under the conquering Normans, and over time the two blended and became equivalent. ã€€Further, the later-imported term became a synonym for the native Old English word sÄ‹Ä«r ([ÊƒiËr]) or, in Modern English, shire â€“ an equivalent administrative division of the kingdom. ã€€The term "county" evolved, consequently, to designate a level of local administration that was immediately beneath a national government, within a unitary (non-federal) system of government. ã€€County later also became used differently in some federal systems of government, for a local administrative division subordinate to a primary subnational entity, such as a Province (e.g. Canada) or a levelÂ 3 territorialÂ unit (NUTSÂ 3). ã€€In the United States and Canada, founded 600 years later on the British traditions, counties are usually an administrative division set by convenient geographical demarcations, which in governance have certain officeholders (for example sheriffs and their departments) as a part of the state and provincial mechanisms, including geographically common court systems. ã€€A county may be further subdivided into districts, hundreds, townships or other administrative jurisdictions within the county. ã€€A county usually, but not always, contains cities, towns, townships, villages, or other municipal corporations, which in most cases are somewhat subordinate or dependent upon county governments. ã€€Depending on the nation, municipality, and local geography, municipalities may or may not be subject to direct or indirect county controlâ€‰â€”â€‰the functions of both levels are often consolidated into a city government when the area is densely populated. ã€€Outside English-speaking countries, an equivalent of the term county is often used to describe subnational jurisdictions that are structurally equivalent to counties in the relationship they have with their national government; but which may not be administratively equivalent to counties in predominantly English-speaking countries.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Model>Model</a> 

<font size=3>
A model is an informative representation of an object, person or system. ã€€The term originally denoted the plans of a building in late 16th-century English, and derived via French and Italian ultimately from Latin modulus, a measure. ã€€Models can be divided into physical models (e.g. a model plane) and abstract models (e.g. mathematical expressions describing behavioural patterns). ã€€Abstract or conceptual models are central to philosophy of science, as almost every scientific theory effectively embeds some kind of model of the physical or human sphere. ã€€In commerce, "model" can refer to a specific design of a product as displayed in a catalogue or show room (e.g. Ford Model T), and by extension to the sold product itself. ã€€Types of models include:

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Former>Former</a> 

<font size=3>
A former is an object, such as a template, gauge or cutting die, which is used to form something such as a boat's hull. ã€€Typically, a former gives shape to a structure that may have complex curvature. ã€€A former may become an integral part of the finished structure, as in an aircraft fuselage, or it may be removable, being using in the construction process and then discarded or re-used.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Party>Party</a> 

<font size=3>
A party is a gathering of people who have been invited by a host for the purposes of socializing, conversation, recreation, or as part of a festival or other commemoration or celebration of a special occasion. ã€€A party will often feature food and beverages, and often conversation, music, dancing, or other forms of entertainment. ã€€Some parties are held in honor of a specific person, day, or event, such as a birthday party, a Super Bowl party, or a St. Patrickâ€™s Day party. ã€€Parties of this kind are often called celebrations. ã€€A party is not necessarily a private occasion. ã€€Public parties are sometimes held in restaurants, pubs, beer gardens, nightclubs, or bars, and people attending such parties may be charged an admission fee by the host. ã€€Large parties in public streets may celebrate events such as Mardi Gras or the signing of a peace treaty ending a long war.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Want>Want</a> 

<font size=3>
The idea of want can be examined from many perspectives. ã€€In secular societies want might be considered similar to the emotion desire, which can be studied scientifically through the disciplines of psychology or sociology. ã€€Want might also be examined in economics as a necessary ingredient in sustaining and perpetuating capitalist societies that are organised around principles like consumerism. ã€€Alternatively want can be studied in a non-secular, spiritual, moralistic or religious way, particularly by Buddhism but also Christianity, Islam and Judaism. ã€€In economics, a want is something that is desired. ã€€It is said that every person has unlimited wants, but limited resources (economics is based on the assumption that only limited resources are available to us). ã€€Thus, people cannot have everything they want and must look for the most affordable alternatives. ã€€Wants are often distinguished from needs. ã€€A need is something that is necessary for survival (such as food and shelter), whereas a want is simply something that a person would like to have. ã€€Some economists have rejected this distinction and maintain that all of these are simply wants, with varying levels of importance. ã€€By this viewpoint, wants and needs can be understood as examples of the overall concept of demand. ã€€Examples of wants that people would like to have is financial monitoring, saving time, higher paying job, more comfort, healthier diet, physical fitness, spirituality, friendship, companionship and safety.
</font>

word | phonetic | definition | translation | root | lemma | degre
---- | ---- | ---- | ---- | ---- | ---- | ----
<font size=2>
<b>idea</b> | ai'diÓ™ | n. the content of cognition; the main thing you are thinking about<br>n. a personal view | <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/idea>n. ä¸»æ„, åŠæ³•, ç†æƒ³, æ€æƒ³, æ¦‚å¿µ, æ„è§<br>[åŒ»] è§‚å¿µ, æ€æƒ³</a> |  |  | 3.93

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thought>Thought</a> 

<font size=3>
In their most common sense, the terms thought and thinking refer to conscious cognitive processes that can happen independently of sensory stimulation. ã€€Their most paradigmatic forms are judging, reasoning, concept formation, problem solving, and deliberation. ã€€But other mental processes, like considering an idea, memory, or imagination, are also often included. ã€€These processes can happen internally independent of the sensory organs, unlike perception. ã€€But when understood in the widest sense, any mental event may be understood as a form of thinking, including perception and unconscious mental processes. ã€€In a slightly different sense, the term thought refers not to the mental processes themselves but to mental states or systems of ideas brought about by these processes. ã€€Various theories of thinking have been proposed, some of which aim to capture the characteristic features of thought. ã€€Platonists hold that thinking consists in discerning and inspecting Platonic forms and their interrelations. ã€€It involves the ability to discriminate between the pure Platonic forms themselves and the mere imitations found in the sensory world. ã€€According to Aristotelianism, to think about something is to instantiate in one's mind the universal essence of the object of thought. ã€€These universals are abstracted from sense experience and are not understood as existing in a changeless intelligible world, in contrast to Platonism. ã€€Conceptualism is closely related to Aristotelianism: it identifies thinking with mentally evoking concepts instead of instantiating essences. ã€€Inner speech theories claim that thinking is a form of inner speech in which words are silently expressed in the thinker's mind. ã€€According to some accounts, this happens in a regular language, like English or French. ã€€The language of thought hypothesis, on the other hand, holds that this happens in the medium of a unique mental language called Mentalese. ã€€Central to this idea is that linguistic representational systems are built up from atomic and compound representations and that this structure is also found in thought. ã€€Associationists understand thinking as the succession of ideas or images. ã€€They are particularly interested in the laws of association that govern how the train of thought unfolds. ã€€Behaviorists, by contrast, identify thinking with behavioral dispositions to engage in public intelligent behavior as a reaction to particular external stimuli. ã€€Computationalism is the most recent of these theories. ã€€It sees thinking in analogy to how computers work in terms of the storage, transmission, and processing of information. ã€€Various types of thinking are discussed in the academic literature. ã€€A judgment is a mental operation in which a proposition is evoked and then either affirmed or denied. ã€€Reasoning, on the other hand, is the process of drawing conclusions from premises or evidence. ã€€Both judging and reasoning depend on the possession of the relevant concepts, which are acquired in the process of concept formation. ã€€In the case of problem solving, thinking aims at reaching a predefined goal by overcoming certain obstacles. ã€€Deliberation is an important form of practical thought that consists in formulating possible courses of action and assessing the reasons for and against them. ã€€This may lead to a decision by choosing the most favorable option. ã€€Both episodic memory and imagination present objects and situations internally, in an attempt to accurately reproduce what was previously experienced or as a free rearrangement, respectively. ã€€Unconscious thought is thought that happens without being directly experienced. ã€€It is sometimes posited to explain how difficult problems are solved in cases where no conscious thought was employed. ã€€Thought is discussed in various academic disciplines. ã€€Phenomenology is interested in the experience of thinking. ã€€An important question in this field concerns the experiential character of thinking and to what extent this character can be explained in terms of sensory experience. ã€€Metaphysics is, among other things, interested in the relation between mind and matter. ã€€This concerns the question of how thinking can fit into the material world as described by the natural sciences. ã€€Cognitive psychology aims to understand thought as a form of information processing. ã€€Developmental psychology, on the other hand, investigates the development of thought from birth to maturity and asks which factors this development depends on. ã€€Psychoanalysis emphasizes the role of the unconscious in mental life. ã€€Other fields concerned with thought include linguistics, neuroscience, artificial intelligence, biology, and sociology. ã€€Various concepts and theories are closely related to the topic of thought. ã€€The term "law of thought" refers to three fundamental laws of logic: the law of contradiction, the law of excluded middle, and the principle of identity. ã€€Counterfactual thinking involves mental representations of non-actual situations and events in which the thinker tries to assess what would be the case if things had been different. ã€€Thought experiments often employ counterfactual thinking in order to illustrate theories or to test their plausibility. ã€€Critical thinking is a form of thinking that is reasonable, reflective, and focused on determining what to believe or how to act. ã€€Positive thinking involves focusing one's attention on the positive aspects of one's situation and is intimately related to optimism.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Father>Father</a> 

<font size=3>
A father is the male parent of a child. ã€€Besides the paternal bonds of a father to his children, the father may have a parental, legal, and social relationship with the child that carries with it certain rights and obligations. ã€€A biological father is the male genetic contributor to the creation of the infant, through sexual intercourse or sperm donation. ã€€A biological father may have legal obligations to a child not raised by him, such as an obligation of monetary support. ã€€An adoptive father is a man who has become the child's parent through the legal process of adoption. ã€€A putative father is a man whose biological relationship to a child is alleged but has not been established. ã€€A stepfather is a male who is the husband of a child's mother and they may form a family unit, but who generally does not have the legal rights and responsibilities of a parent in relation to the child. ã€€The adjective "paternal" refers to a father and comparatively to "maternal" for a mother. ã€€The verb "to father" means to procreate or to sire a child from which also derives the noun "fathering". ã€€Biological fathers determine the sex of their child through a sperm cell which either contains an X chromosome (female), or Y chromosome (male). ã€€Related terms of endearment are dad (dada, daddy), baba, papa, pappa, papasita, (pa, pap) and pop. ã€€A male role model that children can look up to is sometimes referred to as a father-figure.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B>B</a> 

<font size=3>
B, or b, is the second letter of the Latin-script alphabet, used in the modern English alphabet, the alphabets of other western European languages and others worldwide. ã€€Its name in English is bee (pronounced /ËˆbiË/), plural bees. ã€€It represents the voiced bilabial stop in many languages, including English. ã€€In some other languages, it is used to represent other bilabial consonants.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Album>Album</a> 

<font size=3>
An album is a collection of audio recordings issued on compact disc (CD), vinyl, audio tape, or another medium such as digital distribution. ã€€Albums of recorded sound were developed in the early 20th century as individual 78 rpm records collected in a bound book resembling a photograph album; this format evolved after 1948 into single vinyl long-playing (LP) records played at 33+1â„3Â rpm. ã€€The album was the dominant form of recorded music expression and consumption from the mid-1960s to the early 21st century, a period known as the album era. ã€€Vinyl LPs are still issued, though album sales in the 21st-century have mostly focused on CD and MP3 formats. ã€€The 8-track tape was the first tape format widely used alongside vinyl from 1965 until being phased out by 1983 and was gradually supplanted by the cassette tape during the 1970s and early 1980s; the popularity of the cassette reached its peak during the late 1980s, sharply declined during the 1990s and had largely disappeared during the first decade of the 2000s. ã€€Most albums are recorded in a studio, although they may also be recorded in a concert venue, at home, in the field, or a mix of places. ã€€The time frame for completely recording an album varies between a few hours to several years. ã€€This process usually requires several takes with different parts recorded separately, and then brought or "mixed" together. ã€€Recordings that are done in one take without overdubbing are termed "live", even when done in a studio. ã€€Studios are built to absorb sound, eliminating reverberation, to assist in mixing different takes; other locations, such as concert venues and some "live rooms", have reverberation, which creates a "live" sound. ã€€Recordings, including live, may contain editing, sound effects, voice adjustments, etc. ã€€With modern recording technology, artists can be recorded in separate rooms or at separate times while listening to the other parts using headphones; with each part recorded as a separate track. ã€€Album covers and liner notes are used, and sometimes additional information is provided, such as analysis of the recording, and lyrics or librettos. ã€€Historically, the term "album" was applied to a collection of various items housed in a book format. ã€€In musical usage, the word was used for collections of short pieces of printed music from the early nineteenth century. ã€€Later, collections of related 78 rpm records were bundled in book-like albums (one side of a 78 rpm record could hold only about 3.5 minutes of sound). ã€€When LP records were introduced, a collection of pieces or songs on a single record was called an "album"; the word was extended to other recording media such as compact disc, MiniDisc, compact audio cassette, 8-track tape and digital albums as they were introduced.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law>Law</a> 

<font size=3>
Law is a set of rules that are created and are enforceable by social or governmental institutions to regulate behavior, with its precise definition a matter of longstanding debate. ã€€It has been variously described as a science and as the art of justice. ã€€State-enforced laws can be made by a group legislature or by a single legislator, resulting in statutes; by the executive through decrees and regulations; or established by judges through precedent, usually in common law jurisdictions. ã€€Private individuals may create legally binding contracts, including arbitration agreements that adopt alternative ways of resolving disputes to standard court litigation. ã€€The creation of laws themselves may be influenced by a constitution, written or tacit, and the rights encoded therein. ã€€The law shapes politics, economics, history and society in various ways and also serves as a mediator of relations between people. ã€€Legal systems vary between jurisdictions, with their differences analysed in comparative law. ã€€In civil law jurisdictions, a legislature or other central body codifies and consolidates the law. ã€€In common law systems, judges may make binding case law through precedent, although on occasion this may be overturned by a higher court or the legislature. ã€€Historically, religious law has influenced secular matters and is, as of the 21st century, still in use in some religious communities. ã€€Sharia law based on Islamic principles is used as the primary legal system in several countries, including Iran and Saudi Arabia. ã€€The scope of law can be divided into two domains. ã€€Public law concerns government and society, including constitutional law, administrative law, and criminal law. ã€€Private law deals with legal disputes between individuals and/or organisations in areas such as contracts, property, torts/delicts and commercial law. ã€€This distinction is stronger in civil law countries, particularly those with a separate system of administrative courts; by contrast, the public-private law divide is less pronounced in common law jurisdictions. ã€€Law provides a source of scholarly inquiry into legal history, philosophy, economic analysis and sociology. ã€€Law also raises important and complex issues concerning equality, fairness, and justice.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black>Black</a> 

<font size=3>
Black is a color which results from the absence or complete absorption of visible light. ã€€It is an achromatic color, without hue, like white and grey. ã€€It is often used symbolically or figuratively to represent darkness. ã€€Black and white have often been used to describe opposites such as good and evil, the Dark Ages versus Age of Enlightenment, and night versus day. ã€€Since the Middle Ages, black has been the symbolic color of solemnity and authority, and for this reason it is still commonly worn by judges and magistrates. ã€€Black was one of the first colors used by artists in Neolithic cave paintings. ã€€It was used in ancient Egypt and Greece as the color of the underworld. ã€€In the Roman Empire, it became the color of mourning, and over the centuries it was frequently associated with death, evil, witches, and magic. ã€€In the 14th century, it was worn by royalty, clergy, judges, and government officials in much of Europe. ã€€It became the color worn by English romantic poets, businessmen and statesmen in the 19th century, and a high fashion color in the 20th century. ã€€According to surveys in Europe and North America, it is the color most commonly associated with mourning, the end, secrets, magic, force, violence, fear, evil, and elegance. ã€€Black is the most common ink color used for printing books, newspapers and documents, as it provides the highest contrast with white paper and thus is the easiest color to read. ã€€Similarly, black text on a white screen is the most common format used on computer screens. ã€€As of September 2019, the darkest material is made by MIT engineers from vertically aligned carbon nanotubes.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Body_(biology)>Body (biology)</a> 

<font size=3>
A body (Latin: corpus) is the physical material of an organism. ã€€It is only used for organisms which are in one part or whole. ã€€There are organisms which change from single cells to whole organisms: for example, slime molds. ã€€For them the term 'body' would mean the multicellular stage. ã€€Other uses: Plant body: plants are modular , with modules being created by meristems and the body generally consisting of both the shoot system and the root system, with the body's development being influenced by its environment. ã€€Cell body: here it may be used for cells like neurons which have long axons (nerve fibres). ã€€The cell body is the part with the nucleus in it. ã€€The body of a dead person is also called a corpse or cadaver. ã€€The dead bodies of vertebrate animals and insects are sometimes called carcasses. ã€€The human body has a head, neck, torso, two arms, two legs and the genitals of the groin, which differ between males and females. ã€€The branch of biology dealing with the study of the bodies and their specific structural features called morphology. ã€€Anatomy is a branch of morphology that deals with the structure of the body at a level higher than tissue. ã€€Anatomy is closely related to histology, which studies the structure of tissues, as well as cytology, which studies the structure and function of the individual cells, from which the tissues and organs of the studied macroorganism are built. ã€€Taken together, anatomy, histology, cytology and embryology represent a morphology The study of functions and mechanisms in a body is physiology.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Century>Century</a> 

<font size=3>
A century is a period of 100 years. ã€€Centuries are numbered ordinally in English and many other languages. ã€€The word century comes from the Latin centum, meaning one hundred. ã€€Century is sometimes abbreviated as c. ã€€A centennial or centenary is a hundredth anniversary, or a celebration of this, typically the remembrance of an event which took place a hundred years earlier. ã€€A century from now will be 09:33, 13 February 2123.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/College>College</a> 

<font size=3>
A college (Latin: collegium) is an educational institution or a constituent part of one. ã€€A college may be a degree-awarding tertiary educational institution, a part of a collegiate or federal university, an institution offering vocational education, or a secondary school. ã€€In most of the world, a college may be a high school or secondary school, a college of further education, a training institution that awards trade qualifications, a higher-education provider that does not have university status (often without its own degree-awarding powers), or a constituent part of a university. ã€€In the United States, a college may offer undergraduate programs â€“ either as an independent institution or as the undergraduate program of a university â€“ or it may be a residential college of a university or a community college, referring to (primarily public) higher education institutions that aim to provide affordable and accessible education, usually limited to two-year associate degrees. ã€€The word is generally also used as a synonym for a university in the US. ã€€Colleges in countries such as France, Belgium, and Switzerland provide secondary education.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water>Water</a> 

<font size=3>
Water is an inorganic compound with the chemical formula H2O. ã€€It is a transparent, tasteless, odorless, and nearly colorless chemical substance, which is the main constituent of Earth's hydrosphere and the fluids of all known living organisms (in which it acts as a solvent). ã€€It is vital for all known forms of life, despite not providing food, energy or organic micronutrients. ã€€Its chemical formula, H2O, indicates that each of its molecules contains one oxygen and two hydrogen atoms, connected by covalent bonds. ã€€The hydrogen atoms are attached to the oxygen atom at an angle of 104.45Â°. ã€€"Water" is also the name of the liquid state of H2O at standard temperature and pressure. ã€€Because Earth's environment is relatively close to water's triple point, a number of natural states of water exist on earth. ã€€It forms precipitation in the form of rain and aerosols in the form of fog. ã€€Clouds consist of suspended droplets of water and ice, its solid state. ã€€When finely divided, crystalline ice may precipitate in the form of snow. ã€€The gaseous state of water is steam or water vapor. ã€€Water covers about 71% of the Earth's surface, with seas and oceans making up most of the water volume on earth (about 96.5%). ã€€Small portions of water occur as groundwater (1.7%), in the glaciers and the ice caps of Antarctica and Greenland (1.7%), and in the air as vapor, clouds (consisting of ice and liquid water suspended in air), and precipitation (0.001%). ã€€Water moves continually through the water cycle of evaporation, transpiration (evapotranspiration), condensation, precipitation, and runoff, usually reaching the sea. ã€€Water plays an important role in the world economy. ã€€Approximately 70% of the freshwater used by humans goes to agriculture. ã€€Fishing in salt and fresh water bodies has been, and continues to be, a major source of food for many parts of the world, providing 6.5% of global protein. ã€€Much of the long-distance trade of commodities (such as oil, natural gas, and manufactured products) is transported by boats through seas, rivers, lakes, and canals. ã€€Large quantities of water, ice, and steam are used for cooling and heating, in industry and homes. ã€€Water is an excellent solvent for a wide variety of substances both mineral and organic; as such it is widely used in industrial processes, and in cooking and washing. ã€€Water, ice and snow are also central to many sports and other forms of entertainment, such as swimming, pleasure boating, boat racing, surfing, sport fishing, diving, ice skating and skiing.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Building>Building</a> 

<font size=3>
A building, or edifice, is an enclosed structure with a roof and walls standing more or less permanently in one place, such as a house or factory (although there's also portable buildings). ã€€Buildings come in a variety of sizes, shapes, and functions, and have been adapted throughout history for a wide number of factors, from building materials available, to weather conditions, land prices, ground conditions, specific uses, prestige, and aesthetic reasons. ã€€To better understand the term building compare the list of nonbuilding structures. ã€€Buildings serve several societal needs â€“ primarily as shelter from weather, security, living space, privacy, to store belongings, and to comfortably live and work. ã€€A building as a shelter represents a physical division of the human habitat (a place of comfort and safety) and the outside (a place that at times may be harsh and harmful). ã€€Ever since the first cave paintings, buildings have also become objects or canvasses of much artistic expression. ã€€In recent years, interest in sustainable planning and building practices has also become an intentional part of the design process of many new buildings and other structures.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River>River</a> 

<font size=3>
A river is a natural flowing watercourse, usually freshwater stream, flowing on the surface or inside caves towards another waterbody at a lower elevation, such as an ocean, sea, bay, lake, wetland or another river. ã€€In some cases, a river flows into the ground or becomes dry at the end of its course without reaching another body of water. ã€€Small rivers can be referred to using names such as creek, brook and rivulet. ã€€There are no official definitions for the generic term river as applied to geographic features, although in some countries or communities a stream is defined by its size. ã€€Many names for small rivers are specific to geographic location; examples are "run" in some parts of the United States, "burn" in Scotland and Northeast England, and "beck" in Northern England. ã€€Sometimes a river is defined as being larger than a creek, but not always: the language is vague. ã€€Rivers are an important part of the water cycle. ã€€Water from a drainage basin generally collects into a river through surface runoff from precipitation, meltwater released from natural ice and snowpacks, and other underground sources such as groundwater recharge and springs. ã€€Rivers are often considered major features within a landscape; however, they actually only cover around 0.1% of the land on Earth. ã€€Rivers are also an important natural terraformer, as the erosive action of running water with carve out rills, gullies and valleys in the surface, as well as transferring silt and dissolved minerals downstream, forming river deltas and islands where the flow slows down. ã€€As a waterbody, rivers also serve crucial ecological functions by providing and feeding habitats for aquatic and semiaquatic animals and plants, especially for migratory fish species. ã€€Rivers are significant to mankind since many human settlements and civilizations are built around sizeable rivers and streams. ã€€Most of the major cities of the world are situated on the banks of rivers, as they are (or were) depended upon as a vital source of drinking water, for food supply via fishing and agricultural irrigation, for shipping, as natural borders and/or defensive terrains, as a source of hydropower to drive machinery or generate electricity, for bathing, and as a means of disposing of waste. ã€€In the pre-industrial era, larger rivers were a major obstacle to movement of people, goods, and armies across regions. ã€€Towns often developed at the few locations suitable for fording, to build bridges or to support ports, and many major cities such as London are located at the narrowest and most reliable site at which a river could be crossed via bridges or ferries. ã€€In Earth science disciplines, potamology is the scientific study of rivers, while limnology is the study of inland waters in general.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/York>York</a> 

<font size=3>
York is a cathedral city in North Yorkshire, England, with Roman origins, sited at the confluence of the rivers Ouse and Foss. ã€€It is the historic county town of Yorkshire. ã€€The city has many historic buildings and other structures, such as a minster, castle, and city walls. ã€€It is the largest settlement and the administrative centre of the wider City of York district. ã€€The city was founded under the name of Eboracum in 71Â AD. ã€€It then became the capital of the Roman province of Britannia Inferior, and later of the kingdoms of Deira, Northumbria, and Scandinavian York. ã€€In the Middle Ages, it became the northern England ecclesiastical province's centre, and grew as a wool-trading centre. ã€€In the 19th century, it became a major railway network hub and confectionery manufacturing centre. ã€€During the Second World War, part of the Baedeker Blitz bombed the city; it was less affected by the war than other northern cities, with several historic buildings being gutted and restored up to the 1960s. ã€€The city is one of 15 in England to have a lord mayor, and one of two to have The Right Honourable title affixed, the other being London's. ã€€Historic governance of the city was as a county corporate, not included in the county's riding system. ã€€The city has since been covered by a municipal borough, county borough, and since 1996 a non-metropolitan district (the City of York), which also includes surrounding villages and rural areas, and the town of Haxby. ã€€The current district's local council is responsible for providing all local services and facilities throughout this area. ã€€The city had a population of 153,717 in the 2011 census; the wider district had a population of 198,100. ã€€According to 2021[update] census data, the wider district has a population of 202,800, a 2.4% increase compared to the 2011 census.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Room>Room</a> 

<font size=3>
In a building or large vehicle, like a ship, a room is any enclosed space within a number of walls to which entry is possible only via a door or other dividing structure that connects it to either a passageway, another room, or the outdoors, that is large enough for several people to move about, and whose size, fixtures, furnishings, and sometimes placement within the building or ship support the activity to be conducted in it.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Death>Death</a> 

<font size=3>
Death is the irreversible cessation of all biological functions that sustain an organism. ã€€For organisms with a brain, death can also be defined as the irreversible cessation of functioning of the whole brain, including brainstem, and brain death is sometimes used as a legal definition of death. ã€€The remains of a former organism normally begin to decompose shortly after death. ã€€Death is an inevitable process that eventually occurs in almost all organisms. ã€€Death is generally applied to whole organisms; the similar process seen in individual components of an organism, such as cells or tissues, is necrosis. ã€€Something that is not considered an organism, such as a virus, can be physically destroyed, but is not said to die. ã€€As of the early 21st century, over 150,000 humans die each day, with ageing being by far the most common cause of death.ã€€[citation needed] Many cultures and religions have the idea of an afterlife, and also may hold the idea of judgement of good and bad deeds in one's life (heaven, hell, karma).
</font>

---- | ---- | ---- | ---- | ---- | ---- | ----
<font size=2>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data>Data</a> 

<font size=3>
In the pursuit of knowledge, data (US: /ËˆdÃ¦tÉ™/; UK: /ËˆdeÉªtÉ™/) is a collection of discrete values that convey information, describing quantity, quality, fact, statistics, other basic units of meaning, or simply sequences of symbols that may be further interpreted. ã€€A datum is an individual value in a collection of data. ã€€Data is usually organized into structures such as tables that provide additional context and meaning, and which may themselves be used as data in larger structures. ã€€Data may be used as variables in a computational process. ã€€Data may represent abstract ideas or concrete measurements. ã€€Data is commonly used in scientific research, economics, and in virtually every other form of human organizational activity. ã€€Examples of data sets include price indices (such as consumer price index), unemployment rates, literacy rates, and census data. ã€€In this context, data represents the raw facts and figures which can be used in such a manner in order to capture the useful information out of it. ã€€Data is collected using techniques such as measurement, observation, query, or analysis, and typically represented as numbers or characters which may be further processed. ã€€Field data are data that is collected in an uncontrolled in-situ environment. ã€€Experimental data is data that is generated in the course of a controlled scientific experiment. ã€€Data is analyzed using techniques such as calculation, reasoning, discussion, presentation, visualization, or other forms of post-analysis. ã€€Prior to analysis, raw data (or unprocessed data) is typically cleaned: Outliers are removed and obvious instrument or data entry errors are corrected. ã€€Data can be seen as the smallest units of factual information that can be used as a basis for calculation, reasoning, or discussion. ã€€Data can range from abstract ideas to concrete measurements, including but not limited to, statistics. ã€€Thematically connected data presented in some relevant context can be viewed as information. ã€€Contextually connected pieces of information can then be described as data insights or intelligence. ã€€The stock of insights and intelligence that accumulates over time resulting from the synthesis of data into information, can then be described as knowledge. ã€€Data has been described as "the new oil of the digital economy". ã€€Data, as a general concept, refers to the fact that some existing information or knowledge is represented or coded in some form suitable for better usage or processing. ã€€Advances in computing technologies have led to the advent of big data, which usually refers to very large quantities of data, usually at the petabyte scale. ã€€Using traditional data analysis methods and computing, working with such large (and growing) datasets is difficult, even impossible. ã€€(Theoretically speaking, infinite data would yield infinite information, which would render extracting insights or intelligence impossible.) ã€€In response, the relatively new field of data science uses machine learning (and other artificial intelligence (AI)) methods that allow for efficient applications of analytic methods to big data.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Form_(zoology)>Form (zoology)</a> 

<font size=3>
In zoology, the word "form" or forma (literally Latin for form) is a strictly informal term that is sometimes used to describe organisms. ã€€Under the International Code of Zoological Nomenclature the term has no standing (it is not accepted). ã€€In other words, although form names are Latin, and are sometimes wrongly appended to a binomial name, in a zoological context, forms have no taxonomic significance at all.ã€€[citation needed]
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Court>Court</a> 

<font size=3>
A court is any person or institution, often as a government institution, with the authority to adjudicate legal disputes between parties and carry out the administration of justice in civil, criminal, and administrative matters in accordance with the rule of law. ã€€In both common law and civil law legal systems, courts are the central means for dispute resolution, and it is generally understood that all people have an ability to bring their claims before a court. ã€€Similarly, the rights of those accused of a crime include the right to present a defense before a court. ã€€The system of courts that interprets and applies the law is collectively known as the judiciary. ã€€The place where a court sits is known as a venue. ã€€The room where court proceedings occur is known as a courtroom, and the building as a courthouse; court facilities range from simple and very small facilities in rural communities to large complex facilities in urban communities. ã€€The practical authority given to the court is known as its jurisdiction (from Latin iÅ«risdictiÅ, from iÅ«ris, "of the law," + dÄ«cÅ, "to declare," + -tiÅ, noun-forming suffix), the court's power to decide certain kinds of questions or petitions put to it. ã€€According to William Blackstone's Commentaries on the Laws of England, a court (for civil wrongs) is constituted by a minimum of three parties: the Äctor or plaintiff, who complains of an injury done; the reus or defendant, who is called upon to make satisfaction for it; and the jÅ«dex or judicial power, who is to examine the truth of the fact, determine the law arising upon that fact, and, if any injury appears to have been done, ascertain and by its officers apply a legal remedy. ã€€It is also usual in the superior courts to have barristers, and attorneys or counsel, as assistants, though, often, courts consist of additional barristers, bailiffs, reporters, and perhaps a jury. ã€€The term "the court" is also used to refer to the presiding officer or officials, usually one or more judges. ã€€The judge or panel of judges may also be collectively referred to as "the bench" (in contrast to attorneys and barristers, collectively referred to as "the bar"). ã€€In the United States, the legal authority of a court to take action is based on personal jurisdiction over the parties to the litigation and subject-matter jurisdiction over the claims asserted.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/King>King</a> 

<font size=3>
King is the title given to a male monarch in a variety of contexts. ã€€The female equivalent is queen, which title is also given to the consort of a king, although in some cases, the title of King is given to females such as in the case of Mary, Queen of Hungary. ã€€In the context of prehistory, antiquity and contemporary indigenous peoples, the title may refer to tribal kingship. ã€€Germanic kingship is cognate with Indo-European traditions of tribal rulership (c.f. Indic rÄjan, Gothic reiks, and Old Irish rÃ­, etc.). ã€€In the context of classical antiquity, king may translate in Latin as rex and in Greek as archon or basileus. ã€€In classical European feudalism, the title of king as the ruler of a kingdom is understood to be the highest rank in the feudal order, potentially subject, at least nominally, only to an emperor (harking back to the client kings of the Roman Republic and Roman Empire). ã€€In a modern context, the title may refer to the ruler of one of a number of modern monarchies (either absolute or constitutional). ã€€The title of king is used alongside other titles for monarchs: in the West, emperor, grand prince, prince, archduke, duke or grand duke, and in the Islamic world, malik, sultan, emir or hakim, etc. ã€€The city-states of the Aztec Empire had a Tlatoani, which were kings of pre-Hispanic Mesoamerica. ã€€The Huey Tlatoani was the emperor of the Aztecs. ã€€The term king may also refer to a king consort, a title that is sometimes given to the husband of a ruling queen, but the title of prince consort is more common.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History>History</a> 

<font size=3>
History (derived from Ancient Greek á¼±ÏƒÏ„Î¿ÏÎ¯Î± (historÃ­a)Â 'inquiry; knowledge acquired by investigation') is the systematic study and documentation of human activity. ã€€The time period of events before the invention of writing systems is considered prehistory. ã€€"History" is an umbrella term comprising past events as well as the memory, discovery, collection, organization, presentation, and interpretation of these events. ã€€Historians seek knowledge of the past using historical sources such as written documents, oral accounts, art and material artifacts, and ecological markers. ã€€History is not complete and still has debatable mysteries. ã€€History is also an academic discipline which uses narrative to describe, examine, question, and analyze past events, and investigate their patterns of cause and effect. ã€€Historians often debate which narrative best explains an event, as well as the significance of different causes and effects. ã€€Historians also debate the nature of history as an end in itself, as well as its usefulness to give perspective on the problems of the present. ã€€Stories common to a particular culture, but not supported by external sources (such as the tales surrounding King Arthur), are usually classified as cultural heritage or legends. ã€€History differs from myth in that it is supported by verifiable evidence. ã€€However, ancient cultural influences have helped spawn variant interpretations of the nature of history which have evolved over the centuries and continue to change today. ã€€The modern study of history is wide-ranging, and includes the study of specific regions and the study of certain topical or thematic elements of historical investigation. ã€€History is often taught as a part of primary and secondary education, and the academic study of history is a major discipline in university studies. ã€€Herodotus, a 5th-century BC Greek historian, is often considered the "father of history" (as he was one of the first historians) in the Western tradition, although he has also been criticized as the "father of lies". ã€€Along with his contemporary Thucydides, he helped form the foundations for the modern study of past events and societies. ã€€Their works continue to be read today, and the gap between the culture-focused Herodotus and the military-focused Thucydides remains a point of contention or approach in modern historical writing. ã€€In East Asia, a state chronicle, the Spring and Autumn Annals, was reputed to date from as early as 722 BC, although only 2nd-century BC texts have survived.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/March>March</a> 

<font size=3>
March is the third month of the year in both the Julian and Gregorian calendars. ã€€It is the second of seven months to have a length of 31 days. ã€€In the Northern Hemisphere, the meteorological beginning of spring occurs on the first day of March. ã€€The March equinox on the 20 or 21 marks the astronomical beginning of spring in the Northern Hemisphere and the beginning of autumn in the Southern Hemisphere, where September is the seasonal equivalent of the Northern Hemisphere's March.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White>White</a> 

<font size=3>
White is the lightest color and is achromatic (having no hue). ã€€It is the color of objects such as snow, chalk, and milk, and is the opposite of black. ã€€White objects fully reflect and scatter all the visible wavelengths of light. ã€€White on television and computer screens is created by a mixture of red, blue, and green light. ã€€The color white can be given with white pigments, especially titanium dioxide. ã€€In ancient Egypt and ancient Rome, priestesses wore white as a symbol of purity, and Romans wore white togas as symbols of citizenship. ã€€In the Middle Ages and Renaissance a white unicorn symbolized chastity, and a white lamb sacrifice and purity. ã€€It was the royal color of the kings of France, and of the monarchist movement that opposed the Bolsheviks during the Russian Civil War (1917â€“1922). ã€€Greek temples and Roman temples were faced with white marble, and beginning in the 18th century, with the advent of neoclassical architecture, white became the most common color of new churches, capitols and other government buildings, especially in the United States. ã€€It was also widely used in 20th century modern architecture as a symbol of modernity and simplicity. ã€€According to surveys in Europe and the United States, white is the color most often associated with perfection, the good, honesty, cleanliness, the beginning, the new, neutrality, and exactitude. ã€€White is an important color for almost all world religions. ã€€The pope, the head of the Roman Catholic Church, has worn white since 1566, as a symbol of purity and sacrifice. ã€€In Islam, and in the Shinto religion of Japan, it is worn by pilgrims. ã€€In Western cultures and in Japan, white is the most common color for wedding dresses, symbolizing purity and virginity. ã€€In many Asian cultures, white is also the color of mourning.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/September>September</a> 

<font size=3>
September is the ninth month of the year in both the Julian and Gregorian calendars, the third of four months to have a length of 30 days, and the fourth of five months to have a length of fewer than 31 days. ã€€September in the Northern Hemisphere and March in the Southern Hemisphere are seasonally equivalent. ã€€In the Northern hemisphere, the beginning of the meteorological autumn is on 1 September. ã€€In the Southern hemisphere, the beginning of the meteorological spring is on 1 September.ã€€ ã€€September marks the beginning of the ecclesiastical year in the Eastern Orthodox Church. ã€€It is the start of the academic year in many countries of the northern hemisphere, in which children go back to school after the summer break, sometimes on the first day of the month. ã€€September (from Latin septem, "seven") was originally the seventh of ten months in the oldest known Roman calendar, the calendar of Romulus c.â€‰ã€€750 BC, with March (Latin Martius) the first month of the year until perhaps as late as 451 BC. ã€€After the calendar reform that added January and February to the beginning of the year, September became the ninth month but retained its name. ã€€It had 29 days until the Julian reform, which added a day.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Book>Book</a> 

<font size=3>
A book is a medium for recording information in the form of writing or images, typically composed of many pages (made of papyrus, parchment, vellum, or paper) bound together and protected by a cover. ã€€The technical term for this physical arrangement is codex (plural, codices). ã€€In the history of hand-held physical supports for extended written compositions or records, the codex replaces its predecessor, the scroll. ã€€A single sheet in a codex is a leaf and each side of a leaf is a page. ã€€As an intellectual object, a book is prototypically a composition of such great length that it takes a considerable investment of time to compose and still considered as an investment of time to read. ã€€In a restricted sense, a book is a self-sufficient section or part of a longer composition, a usage reflecting that, in antiquity, long works had to be written on several scrolls and each scroll had to be identified by the book it contained. ã€€Each part of Aristotle's Physics is called a book. ã€€In an unrestricted sense, a book is the compositional whole of which such sections, whether called books or chapters or parts, are parts. ã€€The intellectual content in a physical book need not be a composition, nor even be called a book. ã€€Books can consist only of drawings, engravings or photographs, crossword puzzles or cut-out dolls. ã€€In a physical book, the pages can be left blank or can feature an abstract set of lines to support entries, such as in an account book, an appointment book, an autograph book, a notebook, a diary or a sketchbook. ã€€Some physical books are made with pages thick and sturdy enough to support other physical objects, like a scrapbook or photograph album. ã€€Books may be distributed in electronic form as ebooks and other formats. ã€€Although in ordinary academic parlance a monograph is understood to be a specialist academic work, rather than a reference work on a scholarly subject, in library and information science monograph denotes more broadly any non-serial publication complete in one volume (book) or a finite number of volumes (even a novel like Proust's seven-volume In Search of Lost Time), in contrast to serial publications like a magazine, journal or newspaper. ã€€An avid reader or collector of books is a bibliophile or colloquially, "bookworm". ã€€A place where books are traded is a bookshop or bookstore. ã€€Books are also sold elsewhere and can be borrowed from libraries. ã€€Google has estimated that by 2010, approximately 130,000,000 titles had been published. ã€€In some wealthier nations, the sale of printed books has decreased because of the increased usage of ebooks. ã€€Although in most countries printed books continue to outsell their digital counterparts due to many people still preferring to read in a traditional way.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Night>Night</a> 

<font size=3>
Night (also described as nighttime, unconventionally spelled as "nite") is the period of ambient darkness from sunset to sunrise during each 24-hour day, when the Sun is below the horizon. ã€€The exact time when night begins and ends depends on the location and varies throughout the year, based on factors such as season and latitude. ã€€The word can be used in a different sense as the time between bedtime and morning. ã€€In common communication, it is a farewell (sometimes lengthened to "good night"), mainly when someone is going to sleep or leaving. ã€€Astronomical night is the period between astronomical dusk and astronomical dawn when the Sun is between 18 and 90 degrees below the horizon and does not illuminate the sky. ã€€As seen from latitudes between about 48.56Â° and 65.73Â° north or south of the equator, complete darkness does not occur around the summer solstice because, although the Sun sets, it is never more than 18Â° below the horizon at lower culmination, âˆ’90Â° Sun angles occur at the Tropic of Cancer on the December solstice and Tropic of Capricorn on the June solstice, and at the equator on equinoxes. ã€€And as seen from latitudes greater than 72Â° north or south of the equator, complete darkness does not occur in both equinoxes because, although the Sun sets, it is never more than 18Â° below the horizon. ã€€The opposite of night is day (or "daytime", to distinguish it from "day" referring to a 24-hour period). ã€€Twilight is the period of night after sunset or before sunrise when the Sun still illuminates the sky when it is below the horizon. ã€€At any given time, one side of Earth is bathed in sunlight (the daytime), while the other side is in darkness caused by Earth blocking the sunlight. ã€€The central part of the shadow is called the umbra, where the night is darkest. ã€€Natural illumination at night is still provided by a combination of moonlight, planetary light, starlight, zodiacal light, gegenschein, and airglow. ã€€In some circumstances, aurorae, lightning, and bioluminescence can provide some illumination. ã€€The glow provided by artificial lighting is sometimes referred to as light pollution because it can interfere with observational astronomy and ecosystems.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Must>Must</a> 

<font size=3>
Must (from the Latin vinum mustum, "young wine") is freshly crushed fruit juice (usually grape juice) that contains the skins, seeds, and stems of the fruit. ã€€The solid portion of the must is called pomace and typically makes up 7â€“23% of the total weight of the must. ã€€Making must is the first step in winemaking. ã€€Because of its high glucose content, typically between 10 and 15%, must is also used as a sweetener in a variety of cuisines. ã€€Unlike commercially sold grape juice, which is filtered and pasteurized, must is thick with particulate matter, opaque, and comes in various shades of brown and purple.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Present>Present</a> 

<font size=3>
The present (or here and now) is the time that is associated with the events perceived directly and in the first time, not as a recollection (perceived more than once) or a speculation (predicted, hypothesis, uncertain). ã€€It is a period of time between the past and the future, and can vary in meaning from being an instant to a day or longer. ã€€It is sometimes represented as a hyperplane in space-time, typically called "now", although modern physics demonstrates that such a hyperplane cannot be defined uniquely for observers in relative motion. ã€€The present may also be viewed as a duration (see specious present).
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Species>Species</a> 

<font size=3>
In biology, a species is the basic unit of classification and a taxonomic rank of an organism, as well as a unit of biodiversity. ã€€A species is often defined as the largest group of organisms in which any two individuals of the appropriate sexes or mating types can produce fertile offspring, typically by sexual reproduction. ã€€Other ways of defining species include their karyotype, DNA sequence, morphology, behaviour or ecological niche. ã€€In addition, paleontologists use the concept of the chronospecies since fossil reproduction cannot be examined. ã€€The most recent rigorous estimate for the total number of species of eukaryotes is between 8 and 8.7 million. ã€€However, only about 14% of these had been described by 2011. ã€€All species (except viruses) are given a two-part name, a "binomial". ã€€The first part of a binomial is the genus to which the species belongs. ã€€The second part is called the specific name or the specific epithet (in botanical nomenclature, also sometimes in zoological nomenclature). ã€€For example, Boa constrictor is one of the species of the genus Boa, with constrictor being the species' epithet. ã€€While the definitions given above may seem adequate at first glance, when looked at more closely they represent problematic species concepts. ã€€For example, the boundaries between closely related species become unclear with hybridisation, in a species complex of hundreds of similar microspecies, and in a ring species. ã€€Also, among organisms that reproduce only asexually, the concept of a reproductive species breaks down, and each clone is potentially a microspecies. ã€€Although none of these are entirely satisfactory definitions, and while the concept of species may not be a perfect model of life, it is still an incredibly useful tool to scientists and conservationists for studying life on Earth, regardless of the theoretical difficulties. ã€€If species were fixed and clearly distinct from one another, there would be no problem, but evolutionary processes cause species to change. ã€€This obliges taxonomists to decide, for example, when enough change has occurred to declare that a lineage should be divided into multiple chronospecies, or when populations have diverged to have enough distinct character states to be described as cladistic species. ã€€Species were seen from the time of Aristotle until the 18th century as fixed categories that could be arranged in a hierarchy, the great chain of being. ã€€In the 19th century, biologists grasped that species could evolve given sufficient time. ã€€Charles Darwin's 1859 book On the Origin of Species explained how species could arise by natural selection. ã€€That understanding was greatly extended in the 20th century through genetics and population ecology. ã€€Genetic variability arises from mutations and recombination, while organisms themselves are mobile, leading to geographical isolation and genetic drift with varying selection pressures. ã€€Genes can sometimes be exchanged between species by horizontal gene transfer; new species can arise rapidly through hybridisation and polyploidy; and species may become extinct for a variety of reasons. ã€€Viruses are a special case, driven by a balance of mutation and selection, and can be treated as quasispecies.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light>Light</a> 

<font size=3>
Light or visible light is electromagnetic radiation that can be perceived by the human eye. ã€€Visible light is usually defined as having wavelengths in the range of 400â€“700 nanometres (nm), corresponding to frequencies of 750â€“420 terahertz, between the infrared (with longer wavelengths) and the ultraviolet (with shorter wavelengths). ã€€In physics, the term "light" may refer more broadly to electromagnetic radiation of any wavelength, whether visible or not. ã€€In this sense, gamma rays, X-rays, microwaves and radio waves are also light. ã€€The primary properties of light are intensity, propagation direction, frequency or wavelength spectrum and polarization. ã€€Its speed in vacuum, 299792458Â m/s, is one of the fundamental constants of nature. ã€€Like all types of electromagnetic radiation, visible light propagates by massless elementary particles called photons that represents the quanta of electromagnetic field, and can be analyzed as both waves and particles. ã€€The study of light, known as optics, is an important research area in modern physics. ã€€The main source of natural light on Earth is the Sun. ã€€Historically, another important source of light for humans has been fire, from ancient campfires to modern kerosene lamps. ã€€With the development of electric lights and power systems, electric lighting has effectively replaced firelight.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Need>Need</a> 

<font size=3>
A need is dissatisfaction at a point of time and in a given context. ã€€Needs are distinguished from wants. ã€€In the case of a need, a deficiency causes a clear adverse outcome: a dysfunction or death. ã€€In other words, a need is something required for a safe, stable and healthy life (e.g. air, water, food, land, shelter) while a want is a desire, wish or aspiration. ã€€When needs or wants are backed by purchasing power, they have the potential to become economic demands. ã€€Basic needs such as air, water, food and protection from environmental dangers are necessary for an organism to live. ã€€In addition to basic needs, humans also have needs of a social or societal nature such as the human need to socialise or belong to a family unit or group. ã€€Needs can be objective and physical, such as the need for food, or psychical and subjective, such as the need for self-esteem. ã€€The concept of "unmet need" arises in relation to needs in a social context which are not being fulfilled. ã€€Needs and wants are a matter of interest in, and form a common substrate for, the fields of philosophy, biology, psychology, social science, economics, marketing and politics.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Art>Art</a> 

<font size=3>
Art is a diverse range of human activity, and resulting product, that involves creative or imaginative talent expressive of technical proficiency, beauty, emotional power, or conceptual ideas. ã€€There is no generally agreed definition of what constitutes art, and its interpretation has varied greatly throughout history and across cultures. ã€€In the Western tradition, the three classical branches of visual art are painting, sculpture, and architecture. ã€€Theatre, dance, and other performing arts, as well as literature, music, film and other media such as interactive media, are included in a broader definition of the arts. ã€€Until the 17th century, art referred to any skill or mastery and was not differentiated from crafts or sciences. ã€€In modern usage after the 17th century, where aesthetic considerations are paramount, the fine arts are separated and distinguished from acquired skills in general, such as the decorative or applied arts. ã€€The nature of art and related concepts, such as creativity and interpretation, are explored in a branch of philosophy known as aesthetics. ã€€The resulting artworks are studied in the professional fields of art criticism and the history of art.
</font>

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/October>October</a> 

<font size=3>
October is the tenth month of the year in the Julian and Gregorian calendars and the sixth of seven months to have a length of 31 days. ã€€The eighth month in the old calendar of Romulus c.â€‰ã€€750 BC, October retained its name (from Latin and Greek Ã´ctÅ meaning "eight") after January and February were inserted into the calendar that had originally been created by the Romans. ã€€In Ancient Rome, one of three Mundus patet would take place on October 5, Meditrinalia October 11, Augustalia on October 12, October Horse on October 15, and Armilustrium on October 19. ã€€These dates do not correspond to the modern Gregorian calendar. ã€€Among the Anglo-Saxons, it was known as Winterfylleth (Ç·interfylleÃ¾), because at this full moon, winter was supposed to begin. ã€€October is commonly associated with the season of spring in parts of the Southern Hemisphere, and autumn in parts of the Northern Hemisphere, where it is the seasonal equivalent to April in the Southern Hemisphere and vice versa.
</font>

<font size=3>
"Started" is a song recorded by Australian rapper Iggy Azalea for her second studio album In My Defense. ã€€The song was written by Azalea alongside Ronny Wright and produced by American record producer J. White Did It. ã€€It was released by Bad Dreams and Empire on 3 May 2019 as the second single from the album. ã€€It has had over 95 million streams on Spotify becoming the first track from the album to do so. ã€€An accompanying music video for the track, directed by Colin Tilley, was shot the prior month in Los Angeles, California and also premiered that day.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Major>Major</a> 

<font size=3>
Major (commandant in certain jurisdictions) is a military rank of commissioned officer status, with corresponding ranks existing in many armed forces throughout the world. ã€€When used unhyphenated and in conjunction with no other indicators, major is one rank above captain in armies and air forces, and one rank below lieutenant colonel. ã€€It is considered the most junior of the field officer ranks.

## <a href=https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Football>Football</a> 

<font size=3>
Football is a family of team sports that involve, to varying degrees, kicking a ball to score a goal. ã€€Unqualified, the word football normally means the form of football that is the most popular where the word is used. ã€€Sports commonly called football include association football (known as soccer in North America, Ireland and Australia); gridiron football (specifically American football or Canadian football); Australian rules football; rugby union and rugby league; and Gaelic football. ã€€These various forms of football share to varying extent common origins and are known as "football codes". ã€€There are a number of references to traditional, ancient, or prehistoric ball games played in many different parts of the world. ã€€Contemporary codes of football can be traced back to the codification of these games at English public schools during the 19th century. ã€€The expansion and cultural influence of the British Empire allowed these rules of football to spread to areas of British influence outside the directly controlled Empire. ã€€By the end of the 19th century, distinct regional codes were already developing: Gaelic football, for example, deliberately incorporated the rules of local traditional football games in order to maintain their heritage. ã€€In 1888, The Football League was founded in England, becoming the first of many professional football associations. ã€€During the 20th century, several of the various kinds of football grew to become some of the most popular team sports in the world.
</font>
